<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the country. <p><b>Nigeria</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/dʒ/: 'j' in 'jam'">dʒ</span><span title="/ɪər/: 'ear' in 'near'">ɪər</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%"><span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><a href="/wiki/Special:FilePath/En-us-Nigeria.ogg" title="Listen"><img alt="Listen" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span></span></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">ny-<span style="font-size:90%">JEER</span>-ee-ə</i></a>), officially the <b>Federal Republic of Nigeria</b>, is a country in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>.  It is situated between the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> to the north and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Guinea" title="Gulf of Guinea">Gulf of Guinea</a> to the south in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>. It covers an area of 923,769 square kilometres (356,669 sq mi), and with a population of over 218 million, it is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_African_countries_by_population" title="List of African countries by population">most populous country</a> in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_population" title="List of countries and dependencies by population">world's sixth-most populous country</a>. Nigeria borders <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> in <a href="/wiki/Niger%E2%80%93Nigeria_border" title="Niger–Nigeria border">the north</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chad%E2%80%93Nigeria_border" title="Chad–Nigeria border">the northeast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cameroon%E2%80%93Nigeria_border" title="Cameroon–Nigeria border">the east</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">Benin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Benin%E2%80%93Nigeria_border" title="Benin–Nigeria border">the west</a>. Nigeria is a <a href="/wiki/Federation" title="Federation">federal</a> <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a> comprising <a href="/wiki/States_of_Nigeria" title="States of Nigeria">36 states</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Capital_Territory,_Nigeria" title="Federal Capital Territory, Nigeria">Federal Capital Territory</a>, where the capital, <a href="/wiki/Abuja" title="Abuja">Abuja</a>, is located. The <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nigerian_cities_by_population" title="List of Nigerian cities by population">largest city in Nigeria</a> is <a href="/wiki/Lagos" title="Lagos">Lagos</a>, one of the largest <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_cities" title="List of largest cities">metropolitan areas</a> in the world and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Africa_by_population" title="List of cities in Africa by population">second-largest in Africa.</a>
</p><p>Nigeria has been home to several <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_traditional_rulers" title="Nigerian traditional rulers">indigenous pre-colonial states</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sahelian_kingdoms" title="Sahelian kingdoms">kingdoms</a> since the second millennium BC, with the <a href="/wiki/Nok_culture" title="Nok culture">Nok civilization</a> in the 15th century BC, marking the first internal unification in the country. The modern state originated with <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Nigeria" title="Colonial Nigeria">British colonialization</a> in the 19th century, taking its present territorial shape with the merging of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Nigeria_Protectorate" title="Southern Nigeria Protectorate">Southern Nigeria Protectorate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Nigeria_Protectorate" title="Northern Nigeria Protectorate">Northern Nigeria Protectorate</a> in 1914 by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Lugard,_1st_Baron_Lugard" title="Frederick Lugard, 1st Baron Lugard">Lord Lugard</a>. The British set up administrative and legal structures while practising <a href="/wiki/Indirect_rule" title="Indirect rule">indirect rule</a> through <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Chieftaincy" title="Nigerian Chieftaincy">traditional chiefdoms</a> in the Nigeria region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Nigeria became a formally <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Nigeria" title="Federation of Nigeria">independent federation</a> on 1 October, 1960. It experienced a <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Civil_War" title="Nigerian Civil War">civil war from 1967 to 1970</a>, followed by a succession of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nigerian_military_juntas_of_1966%E2%80%9379_and_1983%E2%80%9399" title="Nigerian military juntas of 1966–79 and 1983–99">military dictatorships</a> and democratically elected civilian governments until achieving <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Nigerian_Republic" title="Fourth Nigerian Republic">a stable democracy</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1999_Nigerian_presidential_election" title="1999 Nigerian presidential election">1999 presidential election</a>. The <a href="/wiki/2015_Nigerian_general_election" title="2015 Nigerian general election">2015 general election</a> was the first time an incumbent president would lose re-election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nigeria is a <a href="/wiki/Multinational_state" title="Multinational state">multinational state</a> inhabited by more than <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Nigeria" title="Demographics of Nigeria">250 ethnic groups</a> speaking <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Nigeria" title="Languages of Nigeria">500 distinct languages</a>, all identifying with a wide variety of cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The three largest ethnic groups are the <a href="/wiki/Hausa_people" title="Hausa people">Hausa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Region,_Nigeria" title="Northern Region, Nigeria">the north</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_people" title="Yoruba people">Yoruba</a> in <a href="/wiki/Yorubaland" title="Yorubaland">the west</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Igbo_people" title="Igbo people">Igbo</a> in <a href="/wiki/Igboland" title="Igboland">the east</a>, together comprising over 60% of the total population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> is <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_English" title="Nigerian English">English</a>, chosen to facilitate linguistic unity at the national level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Nigeria" title="Constitution of Nigeria">Nigeria's constitution</a> ensures <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">freedom of religion</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and it is home to some of the world's largest <a href="/wiki/Islam_by_country" title="Islam by country">Muslim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity_by_country" title="Christianity by country">Christian</a> populations, simultaneously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Nigeria is divided roughly in half between Muslims, who live mostly in the <a href="/wiki/North" title="North">north</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a>, who live mostly in the south; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_African_religions" title="Traditional African religions">indigenous religions</a>, such as those native to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odinani" title="Odinani">Igbo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_religion" title="Yoruba religion">Yoruba</a> ethnicities, are in the minority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USEN_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-USEN-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nigeria is a <a href="/wiki/Regional_power" title="Regional power">regional power</a> in Africa, a <a href="/wiki/Middle_power" title="Middle power">middle power</a> in international affairs, and is an <a href="/wiki/Emerging_power" title="Emerging power">emerging global power</a>. Nigeria's <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Nigeria" title="Economy of Nigeria">economy</a> is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_African_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of African countries by GDP (nominal)">largest in Africa</a>, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal)">31st-largest in the world</a> by nominal <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP)">26th-largest</a> by <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">PPP</a>. Nigeria is often referred to as the Giant of Africa owing to its large population and economy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and is considered to be an <a href="/wiki/Emerging_market" title="Emerging market">emerging market</a> by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>. However, the country ranks very low in the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> and remains <a href="/wiki/Corruption_in_Nigeria" title="Corruption in Nigeria">one of the most corrupt nations</a> in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Nigeria is a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a> and a member of many international organizations, including the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">NAM</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Economic_Community_of_West_African_States" title="Economic Community of West African States">Economic Community of West African States</a>, and <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a>. It is also a member of the informal <a href="/wiki/MINT_(economics)" title="MINT (economics)">MINT</a> group of countries and is one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Next_Eleven" title="Next Eleven">Next Eleven</a> economies.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The name <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Nigeria" title="wikt:Nigeria">Nigeria</a></i> was taken from the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a> running through the country. This name was coined on 8 January 1897, by British journalist <a href="/wiki/Flora_Shaw,_Lady_Lugard" title="Flora Shaw, Lady Lugard">Flora Shaw</a>, who later married <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Lugard,_1st_Baron_Lugard" title="Frederick Lugard, 1st Baron Lugard">Lord Lugard</a>, a <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British colonial</a> administrator. The neighbouring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Niger" title="Republic of Niger">Republic of Niger</a> takes its name from the same river. The origin of the name <i>Niger</i>, which originally applied to only the middle reaches of the Niger River, is uncertain. The word is likely an alteration of the <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg</a> name <i>egerew <u>n-iger</u>ewen</i> used by inhabitants along the middle reaches of the river around <a href="/wiki/Timbuktu" title="Timbuktu">Timbuktu</a> before 19th-century European colonialism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Nigeria" title="Demographics of Nigeria">Demographics of Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Population_density_map_of_Nigerian_states_-_English.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="638" data-file-width="823" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Population_density_map_of_Nigerian_states_-_English.png/310px-Population_density_map_of_Nigerian_states_-_English.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Population_density_map_of_Nigerian_states_-_English.png/465px-Population_density_map_of_Nigerian_states_-_English.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Population_density_map_of_Nigerian_states_-_English.png/620px-Population_density_map_of_Nigerian_states_-_English.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Population_density_map_of_Nigerian_states_-_English.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Population density (persons per square kilometer) in Nigeria</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> estimates that the population of Nigeria in 2021 was at 213,401,323<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2022_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP_2022-27">[27]</a></sup>, distributed as 51.7% rural and 48.3% urban, and with a population density of 167.5 people per square kilometre. Around 42.5% of the population were 14 years or younger, 19.6% were aged 15–24, 30.7% were aged 25–54, 4.0% were aged 55–64, and 3.1% were aged 65 years or older. The median age in 2017 was 18.4 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-People_and_Society:_Population22_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-People_and_Society:_Population22-28">[28]</a></sup> Nigeria is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_population#Sovereign_states_and_dependencies_by_population" title="List of countries and dependencies by population">world's sixth-most populous country</a>. The birth rate is 35.2-births/1,000 population and the death rate is 9.6 deaths/1,000 population as of 2017, while the total fertility rate is 5.07 children born/woman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-People_and_Society:_Population22_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-People_and_Society:_Population22-28">[28]</a></sup> Nigeria's population increased by 57 million from 1990 to 2008, a 60% growth rate in less than two decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEApop20112_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEApop20112-29">[29]</a></sup> Nigeria is the most populous country in Africa and accounts for about 17% of the continent's total population as of 2017; however, exactly how populous is a subject of speculation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto12_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto12-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>National census results in the past few decades have been disputed. The results of the most recent census were released in December 2006 and gave a population of 140,003,542. The only breakdown available was by gender: males numbered 71,709,859, females numbered 68,293,008. According to the United Nations, Nigeria has been undergoing explosive population growth and has one of the highest growth and fertility rates in the world. By their projections, Nigeria is one of eight countries expected to account collectively for half of the world's total population increase in 2005–2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The UN estimates that by 2100 the Nigerian population will be between 505 million and 1.03 billion people (middle estimate: 730 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> In 1950, Nigeria had only 33 million people. In 2012, President Goodluck Jonathan said Nigerians should limit their number of children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Millions of Nigerians have emigrated during times of economic hardship, primarily to Europe, North America and Australia. It is estimated that over a million Nigerians have emigrated to the United States and constitute the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nigerian_American" title="Nigerian American">Nigerian American</a> populace. Individuals in many such Diasporic communities have joined the "Egbe Omo Yoruba" society, a national association of Yoruba descendants in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Nigeria's largest city is <a href="/wiki/Lagos" title="Lagos">Lagos</a>. Lagos has grown from about 300,000 in 1950<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> to an estimated 13.4 million in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Major_Urban_Areas:_Population_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Major_Urban_Areas:_Population-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_ethnic_groups_in_Nigeria" title="List of ethnic groups in Nigeria">List of ethnic groups in Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="PieChartTemplate thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px">
<div class="mw-no-invert" style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden;border-radius:100px;border:1px solid black;transform:scaleX(-1)rotate(-90deg)">
<div style="border:solid transparent;background-color: initial;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:0; top:0; border-width:0 200px 200px 0; border-color:White"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;background-color: initial;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 33.188051739324px; border-left-color:Cyan"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:Cyan"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:Cyan"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;background-color: initial;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 21.036851205653px; border-left-color:Black"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:Black"></div>

</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<p>Ethnicity in Nigeria (2020)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Africa:_Nigeria_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Africa:_Nigeria-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:Green; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Hausa_people" title="Hausa people">Hausa</a> (25%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:Red; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_people" title="Yoruba people">Yoruba</a> (21%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:Blue; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Igbo_people" title="Igbo people">Igbo</a> (18%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:Orange; color:black;"> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fulani_people" title="Fulani people">Fulani</a> (6%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:Pink; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Ibibio_people" title="Ibibio people">Ibibio</a> (3.5%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:Maroon; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Tiv_people" title="Tiv people">Tiv</a> (2.4%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:Black; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Kanuri_people" title="Kanuri people">Kanuri</a> (2.4%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:Cyan; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Ijaw_people" title="Ijaw people">Ijaw</a> (1.8%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:White; color:black;"> </span> Others (19.9%)</div>
</div>
</div></div>
<p>Nigeria has more than 250 ethnic groups, with varying languages and customs, creating a country of rich ethnic diversity. The three largest ethnic groups are the <a href="/wiki/Hausa_people" title="Hausa people">Hausa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_people" title="Yoruba people">Yoruba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Igbo_people" title="Igbo people">Igbo</a>, together accounting for more than 60% of the population, while the <a href="/wiki/Edo_people" title="Edo people">Edo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ijaw_people" title="Ijaw people">Ijaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fula_people" title="Fula people">Fulɓe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kanuri_people" title="Kanuri people">Kanuri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urhobo_people" title="Urhobo people">Urhobo-Isoko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibibio_people" title="Ibibio people">Ibibio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ebira_people" title="Ebira people">Ebira</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nupe_people" title="Nupe people">Nupe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gbagyi_people" title="Gbagyi people">Gbagyi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jukun_people_(West_Africa)" title="Jukun people (West Africa)">Jukun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Igala_people" title="Igala people">Igala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Idoma_people" title="Idoma people">Idoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ogoni_people" title="Ogoni people">Ogoni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tiv_people" title="Tiv people">Tiv</a> comprise between 35 and 40%; other minorities make up the remaining 5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGeo_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-NGeo-38">[38]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Middle_Belt" title="Middle Belt">Middle Belt</a> of Nigeria is known for its diversity of ethnic groups, including the <a href="/wiki/Atyap_people" title="Atyap people">Atyap</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berom_people" title="Berom people">Berom</a>, Goemai, Igala, <a href="/wiki/Kofyar_people" title="Kofyar people">Kofyar</a>, Pyem, and <a href="/wiki/Tiv_people" title="Tiv people">Tiv</a>. The official population count of each of Nigeria's ethnicities is disputed as members of different ethnic groups believe the census is rigged to give a particular group (usually believed to be northern groups) numerical superiority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-felix2_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-felix2-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are small minorities of British, American, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-resident_Indian_and_person_of_Indian_origin" title="Non-resident Indian and person of Indian origin">Indian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people_in_Nigeria" title="Chinese people in Nigeria">Chinese</a> (est. 50,000),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/White_Zimbabweans" title="White Zimbabweans">white Zimbabwean</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Japanese, Greek, Syrian and Lebanese immigrants. Immigrants also include those from other West African or East African nations. These minorities mostly reside in major cities such as Lagos and Abuja, or the Niger Delta as employees for the major oil companies. Several Cubans settled in Nigeria as political refugees following the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Revolution" title="Cuban Revolution">Cuban Revolution</a>.
</p><p>In the middle of the 19th century, several ex-slaves of <a href="/wiki/Afro-Cubans" title="Afro-Cubans">Afro-Cuban</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afro-Brazilians" title="Afro-Brazilians">Afro-Brazilian</a> descent<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> and emigrants from Sierra Leone established communities in Lagos and other regions of Nigeria. Many ex-slaves came to Nigeria following the emancipation of slaves in the Americas. Many of the immigrants, sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Saro_people" title="Saro people">Saro</a> (immigrants from Sierra Leone) and <a href="/wiki/Brazilians_in_Nigeria" title="Brazilians in Nigeria">Amaro</a> (ex-slaves from Brazil)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> later became prominent merchants and missionaries in these cities.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Nigeria" title="Languages of Nigeria">Languages of Nigeria</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nigeria_linguistical_map_1979.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="734" data-file-width="954" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Nigeria_linguistical_map_1979.svg/320px-Nigeria_linguistical_map_1979.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Nigeria_linguistical_map_1979.svg/480px-Nigeria_linguistical_map_1979.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Nigeria_linguistical_map_1979.svg/640px-Nigeria_linguistical_map_1979.svg.png 2x" width="320"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nigeria_linguistical_map_1979.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Nigeria's linguistic groups</div></div></div>
<p>525 languages have been spoken in Nigeria; eight of them are extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> In some areas of Nigeria, ethnic groups speak more than one language. The official language of Nigeria, English, was chosen to facilitate the cultural and linguistic unity of the country, owing to the influence of British colonisation which ended in 1960. Many French speakers from surrounding countries have influenced the English spoken in the border regions of Nigeria and some Nigerian citizens have become fluent enough in French to work in the surrounding countries. The French spoken in Nigeria may be mixed with some native languages and English.
</p><p>The major languages spoken in Nigeria represent three major families of <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Africa" title="Languages of Africa">languages of Africa</a>: the majority are <a href="/wiki/Niger%E2%80%93Congo_languages" title="Niger–Congo languages">Niger-Congo</a> languages, such as <a href="/wiki/Igbo_language" title="Igbo language">Igbo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_language" title="Yoruba language">Yoruba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibibio_language" title="Ibibio language">Ibibio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ijaw_languages" title="Ijaw languages">Ijaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fula_language" title="Fula language">Fulfulde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ogoni_languages" title="Ogoni languages">Ogoni</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Edo_language" title="Edo language">Edo</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kanuri_language" title="Kanuri language">Kanuri</a>, spoken in the northeast, primarily in <a href="/wiki/Borno_State" title="Borno State">Borno</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yobe_State" title="Yobe State">Yobe State</a>, is part of the <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilo-Saharan</a> family, and <a href="/wiki/Hausa_language" title="Hausa language">Hausa</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic</a> language. Even though most ethnic groups prefer to communicate in their languages, English as the official language is widely used for education, business transactions and official purposes. English as a first language is used by only a small minority of the country's urban elite, and it is not spoken at all in some rural areas. Hausa is the most widely spoken of the three main languages spoken in Nigeria.
</p><p>With the majority of Nigeria's populace in the rural areas, the major languages of communication in the country remain indigenous languages. Some of the largest of these, notably Yoruba and Igbo, have derived standardised languages from several different dialects and are widely spoken by those ethnic groups. <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Pidgin" title="Nigerian Pidgin">Nigerian Pidgin English</a>, often known simply as "<a href="/wiki/Pidgin" title="Pidgin">Pidgin</a>" or "Broken" (Broken English), is also a popular <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a>, though with varying regional influences on dialect and slang. The pidgin English or Nigerian English is widely spoken within the Niger Delta Region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Nigeria" title="Religion in Nigeria">Religion in Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Catedral_Nacional_em_Abuja,_Nig%C3%A9ria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="945" data-file-width="719" decoding="async" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Catedral_Nacional_em_Abuja%2C_Nig%C3%A9ria.jpg/220px-Catedral_Nacional_em_Abuja%2C_Nig%C3%A9ria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Catedral_Nacional_em_Abuja%2C_Nig%C3%A9ria.jpg/330px-Catedral_Nacional_em_Abuja%2C_Nig%C3%A9ria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Catedral_Nacional_em_Abuja%2C_Nig%C3%A9ria.jpg/440px-Catedral_Nacional_em_Abuja%2C_Nig%C3%A9ria.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Catedral_Nacional_em_Abuja,_Nig%C3%A9ria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Church_of_Nigeria" title="National Church of Nigeria">National Church of Nigeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abuja" title="Abuja">Abuja</a></div></div></div><p>Nigeria is a religiously diverse society, with <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> being the most widely professed religions. Nigerians are nearly equally divided into <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a>, with a tiny minority of adherents of <a href="/wiki/Traditional_African_religions" title="Traditional African religions">traditional African religions</a> and other religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cia_19_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cia_19-49">[49]</a></sup> The Christian share of Nigeria's population is on the decline because of the lower <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">fertility rate</a> compared to Muslims in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> As in other parts of Africa where Islam and Christianity are dominant, religious syncretism with the traditional African religions is common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2012 report on religion and public life by the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> stated that in 2010, 49.3 per cent of Nigeria's population was Christian, 48.8 per cent was Muslim, and 1.9 per cent were followers of indigenous and other religions or unaffiliated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pew_Forum_on_Religion2_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pew_Forum_on_Religion2-52">[52]</a></sup> However, in a report released by Pew Research Center in 2015, the Muslim population was estimated to be 50%, and by 2060, according to the report, Muslims will account for about 60% of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The 2010 census of <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Religion_Data_Archives" title="Association of Religion Data Archives">Association of Religion Data Archives</a> has also reported that 48.8% of the total population was Christian, slightly larger than the Muslim population of 43.4%, while 7.5% were members of other religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto2_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-54">[54]</a></sup> However, these estimates should be taken with caution because sample data is mostly collected from major urban areas in the south, which are predominantly Christian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Regional_Distribution_of_Christians2_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Regional_Distribution_of_Christians2-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Islam dominates North-Western Nigeria (Hausa, Fulani and others), with 99% Muslim, and Northern Eastern Nigeria (Kanuri, Fulani and other groups). In the west, the Yoruba tribe is predominantly of mixed Muslim and Christian backgrounds, with a few adherents of traditional religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> and locally cultivated Christianity are widely practised in Western areas, while <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholicism</a> is a more prominent Christian feature of South Eastern Nigeria. Both Roman Catholicism and Protestantism are observed in the Ibibio, <a href="/wiki/Efik_people" title="Efik people">Efik</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ijaw_people" title="Ijaw people">Ijo</a> and Ogoni lands of the south. The Igbos (predominant in the east) and the <a href="/wiki/Ibibio_people" title="Ibibio people">Ibibio</a> (south) are 98% Christian, with 2% practising traditional religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Middle_Belt" title="Middle Belt">middle belt</a> of Nigeria contains the largest number of minority ethnic groups in Nigeria, who were found to be mostly Christians and members of traditional religions, with a small proportion of Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NG-Sharia.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="355" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/NG-Sharia.png/220px-NG-Sharia.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/NG-Sharia.png/330px-NG-Sharia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/NG-Sharia.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NG-Sharia.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nigerian states that implement some form of sharia law (in green)</div></div></div><p>Nigeria has the largest Muslim population in sub-Saharan Africa. The vast majority of <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Nigeria" title="Islam in Nigeria">Muslims in Nigeria</a> are <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a> belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Maliki" title="Maliki">Maliki</a> school of <a href="/wiki/Jurisprudence" title="Jurisprudence">jurisprudence</a>; however, a sizeable minority also belongs to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shafi%CA%BDi_school" title="Shafiʽi school">Shafi</a> <a href="/wiki/Madhhab" title="Madhhab">Madhhab</a>. A large number of Sunni Muslims are members of <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> brotherhoods. Most Sufis follow the <a href="/wiki/Qadiriyya" title="Qadiriyya">Qadiriyya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tijaniyyah" title="Tijaniyyah">Tijaniyyah</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Mouride" title="Mouride">Mouride</a> movements. A significant <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam_in_Nigeria" title="Shia Islam in Nigeria">Shia minority exists</a>. Some northern states have incorporated <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> law into their previously secular legal systems, which has brought about some controversy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tiptoe_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-tiptoe-61">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kano_State" title="Kano State">Kano State</a> has sought to incorporate Sharia law into its constitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The majority of <a href="/wiki/Quranism" title="Quranism">Quranists</a> follow the Kalo Kato or Quraniyyun movement. There are also <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadiyya</a> and Mahdiyya minorities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diversity_in_Nigerian_Islam_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Diversity_in_Nigerian_Islam-63">[63]</a></sup> as well as followers of the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Baháʼí_Community_of_Nigeria_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Baháʼí_Community_of_Nigeria-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among Christians, the Pew Research survey found that 74% were Protestant, 25% were Catholic, and 1% belonged to other Christian denominations, including a small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Christian" title="Orthodox Christian">Orthodox Christian</a> community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-features.pewforum.org2_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-features.pewforum.org2-66">[66]</a></sup> Leading Protestant churches in the country include the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Nigeria" title="Church of Nigeria">Church of Nigeria</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a>, the <a href="/wiki/General_Council_of_the_Assemblies_of_God_Nigeria" title="General Council of the Assemblies of God Nigeria">Assemblies of God Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Baptist_Convention" title="Nigerian Baptist Convention">Nigerian Baptist Convention</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T.B._Joshua" title="T.B. Joshua">The Synagogue, Church Of All Nations</a>. Since the 1990s, there has been significant growth in many other churches, independently started in Africa by Africans, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">evangelical Protestant</a> ones. These include the <a href="/wiki/Redeemed_Christian_Church_of_God" title="Redeemed Christian Church of God">Redeemed Christian Church of God</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Winners%27_Chapel" title="Winners' Chapel">Winners' Chapel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christ_Apostolic_Church" title="Christ Apostolic Church">Christ Apostolic Church</a> (the first Aladura Movement in Nigeria), <a href="/wiki/Living_Faith_Church_Worldwide" title="Living Faith Church Worldwide">Living Faith Church Worldwide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deeper_Christian_Life_Ministry" title="Deeper Christian Life Ministry">Deeper Christian Life Ministry</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical_Church_of_West_Africa" title="Evangelical Church of West Africa">Evangelical Church of West Africa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mountain_of_Fire_and_Miracles" title="Mountain of Fire and Miracles">Mountain of Fire and Miracles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christ_Embassy" title="Christ Embassy">Christ Embassy</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Lord%27s_Chosen_Charismatic_Revival_Movement" title="The Lord's Chosen Charismatic Revival Movement">Lord's Chosen Charismatic Revival Movement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celestial_Church_of_Christ" title="Celestial Church of Christ">Celestial Church of Christ</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dominion_City" title="Dominion City">Dominion City</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints_in_Nigeria" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints in Nigeria">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aladura_Church" title="Aladura Church">Aladura Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Seventh-day_Adventist_Church_in_Nigeria" title="Seventh-day Adventist Church in Nigeria">Seventh-day Adventist</a> and various indigenous churches have also experienced growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The Yoruba area contains a large Anglican population, while <a href="/wiki/Igboland" title="Igboland">Igboland</a> is a mix of Roman Catholics, Protestants, and a small population of <a href="/wiki/Igbo_Jews" title="Igbo Jews">Igbo Jews</a>. The Edo area is composed predominantly of members of the Assemblies of God, which was introduced into Nigeria by Augustus Ehurie Wogu and his associates at Old Umuahia.
</p><p>Nigeria has become an African hub for the <a href="/wiki/Grail_Movement" title="Grail Movement">Grail Movement</a> and the <a href="/wiki/International_Society_for_Krishna_Consciousness" title="International Society for Krishna Consciousness">Hare Krishnas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> and the largest temple of the <a href="/wiki/Eckankar" title="Eckankar">Eckankar</a> religion is in Port Harcourt, Rivers State, with a total capacity of 10,000.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Nigeria" title="History of Nigeria">History of Nigeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Nigerian_history" title="Timeline of Nigerian history">Timeline of Nigerian history</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Early_history_of_Nigeria" title="Early history of Nigeria">Early history of Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:191px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="1660" decoding="async" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg/189px-Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg/284px-Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg/378px-Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg 2x" width="189"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nok" title="Nok">Nok</a> sculpture, <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracotta</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kainji_Dam" title="Kainji Dam">Kainji Dam</a> excavations showed ironworking by the 2nd century BC. The transition from <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> times to the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> was accomplished without intermediate <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">bronze</a> production. Others believe or suggest the technology moved west from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" title="Nile Valley">Nile Valley</a>, although the Iron Age in the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a> valley and the forest region appears to predate the introduction of metallurgy in the upper savanna by more than 800 years.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Nok_culture" title="Nok culture">Nok civilization</a> of Nigeria thrived between 1,500 BC and AD 200. It produced life-sized <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracotta</a> figures that are some of the earliest known sculptures in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PB_2014_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-PB_2014-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archaeology_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-archaeology-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and smelted iron by about 550 BC and possibly a few centuries earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Evidence of iron smelting has also been excavated at sites in the <a href="/wiki/Nsukka" title="Nsukka">Nsukka</a> region of southeast Nigeria: dating to 2000 BC at the site of <a href="/wiki/Lejja" title="Lejja">Lejja</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eze-Uzomaka_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eze-Uzomaka-79">[79]</a></sup> and to 750 BC and at the site of <a href="/wiki/Opi_(archaeological_site)" title="Opi (archaeological site)">Opi</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Nigeria_before_1500" title="History of Nigeria before 1500">History of Nigeria before 1500</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Intricate_bronze_ceremonial_pot,_9th_century,_Igbo-Ukwu,_Nigeria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2740" data-file-width="1993" decoding="async" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Intricate_bronze_ceremonial_pot%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu%2C_Nigeria.jpg/220px-Intricate_bronze_ceremonial_pot%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu%2C_Nigeria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Intricate_bronze_ceremonial_pot%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu%2C_Nigeria.jpg/330px-Intricate_bronze_ceremonial_pot%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu%2C_Nigeria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Intricate_bronze_ceremonial_pot%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu%2C_Nigeria.jpg/440px-Intricate_bronze_ceremonial_pot%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu%2C_Nigeria.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Intricate_bronze_ceremonial_pot,_9th_century,_Igbo-Ukwu,_Nigeria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ceremonial Igbo pot from 9th-century <a href="/wiki/Igbo-Ukwu" title="Igbo-Ukwu">Igbo-Ukwu</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Kano_Chronicle" title="Kano Chronicle">Kano Chronicle</a></i> highlights an ancient history dating to around 999 AD of the <a href="/wiki/Hausa_Kingdoms" title="Hausa Kingdoms">Hausa</a> <a href="/wiki/Sahelian_kingdoms" title="Sahelian kingdoms">Sahelian city-state</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kano_(city)" title="Kano (city)">Kano</a>, with other major Hausa cities (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hausa_Bakwai" title="Hausa Bakwai">Hausa Bakwai</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Daura" title="Daura">Daura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hadejia" title="Hadejia">Hadeija</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kano_(city)" title="Kano (city)">Kano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Katsina" title="Katsina">Katsina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zazzau" title="Zazzau">Zazzau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rano" title="Rano">Rano</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gobir" title="Gobir">Gobir</a> all having recorded histories dating back to the 10th century. With the spread of Islam from the 7th century AD, the area became known as <i><a href="/wiki/Sudan_(region)" title="Sudan (region)">Sudan</a></i> or as <i>Bilad Al Sudan</i> (English: Land of the Blacks; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: بلاد السودان). Since the populations were partially affiliated with the Arab Muslim culture of <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, they began <a href="/wiki/Trans-Saharan_trade" title="Trans-Saharan trade">Trans-Saharan trade</a> and were referred to by the Arabic speakers as <i>Al-Sudan</i> (meaning "The Blacks") as they were considered an extended part of the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a>. There are early historical references by medieval Arab and Muslim historians and geographers which refer to the <a href="/wiki/Kanem%E2%80%93Bornu_Empire" title="Kanem–Bornu Empire">Kanem-Bornu Empire</a> as the region's major centre for Islamic civilization.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Nri" title="Kingdom of Nri">Kingdom of Nri</a> of the Igbo people consolidated in the 10th century and continued until it lost its sovereignty to the British in 1911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Juang3_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Juang3-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Nri was ruled by the <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Nri" title="List of rulers of Nri">Eze Nri</a>, and the city of Nri is considered to be the foundation of Igbo culture. Nri and <a href="/wiki/Aguleri" title="Aguleri">Aguleri</a>, where the Igbo creation myth originates, are in the territory of the Umeuri clan. Members of the clan trace their lineages back to the patriarchal king-figure <a href="/wiki/Eri_(king)" title="Eri (king)">Eri</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> In West Africa, the oldest bronzes made using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lost_wax_process" title="Lost wax process">lost wax process</a> were from <a href="/wiki/Igbo-Ukwu" title="Igbo-Ukwu">Igbo-Ukwu</a>, a city under Nri influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Juang3_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Juang3-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>
The <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_people" title="Yoruba people">Yoruba</a> kingdoms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ife" title="Ife">Ife</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oyo_Empire" title="Oyo Empire">Oyo</a> in southwestern Nigeria became prominent in the 12th<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Falola13_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Falola13-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> and 14th<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> centuries, respectively. The oldest signs of <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> settlement at Ife's current site date back to the 9th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Falola13_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Falola13-83">[83]</a></sup> and its material culture includes <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracotta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> figures.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yoruba-bronze-head.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="386" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Yoruba-bronze-head.jpg/220px-Yoruba-bronze-head.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1d/Yoruba-bronze-head.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yoruba-bronze-head.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Yoruba_people" title="Yoruba people">Yoruba</a> copper mask of <a href="/wiki/Obalufon_Alayemore" title="Obalufon Alayemore">Obalufon</a> from the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ife" title="Ife">Ife</a> <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1300</span></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-colonial_era">Pre-colonial era</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Nigeria_(1500%E2%80%931800)" title="History of Nigeria (1500–1800)">History of Nigeria (1500–1800)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Queen_Mother_Pendant_Mask-_Iyoba_MET_DP231460.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1483" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Queen_Mother_Pendant_Mask-_Iyoba_MET_DP231460.jpg/170px-Queen_Mother_Pendant_Mask-_Iyoba_MET_DP231460.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Queen_Mother_Pendant_Mask-_Iyoba_MET_DP231460.jpg/255px-Queen_Mother_Pendant_Mask-_Iyoba_MET_DP231460.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Queen_Mother_Pendant_Mask-_Iyoba_MET_DP231460.jpg/340px-Queen_Mother_Pendant_Mask-_Iyoba_MET_DP231460.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Queen_Mother_Pendant_Mask-_Iyoba_MET_DP231460.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Royal <a href="/wiki/Benin_ivory_mask" title="Benin ivory mask">Benin ivory mask</a>, one of Nigeria's most recognized artifacts. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benin_Empire" title="Benin Empire">Benin Empire</a>, 16th century.</div></div></div>
<p>In the 16th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portuguese_discoveries" title="Portuguese discoveries">Portuguese explorers</a> were the first Europeans to begin important, direct trade with peoples of southern Nigeria, at the port they named Lagos (formerly Eko) and in <a href="/wiki/Calabar" title="Calabar">Calabar</a> along the region <a href="/wiki/Slave_Coast_of_West_Africa" title="Slave Coast of West Africa">Slave Coast</a>. Europeans traded goods with peoples at the coast; coastal trade with Europeans also marked the beginnings of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diverse-slavery_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-diverse-slavery-86">[86]</a></sup> The port of Calabar on the historical <a href="/wiki/Bight_of_Biafra" title="Bight of Biafra">Bight of Biafra</a> (now commonly referred to as the Bight of Bonny) became one of the largest slave-trading posts in West Africa in the era of the transatlantic slave trade. Other major slaving ports in Nigeria were located in <a href="/wiki/Badagry" title="Badagry">Badagry</a>, Lagos on the <a href="/wiki/Bight_of_Benin" title="Bight of Benin">Bight of Benin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bonny_Island" title="Bonny Island">Bonny Island</a> on the Bight of Biafra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diverse-slavery_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-diverse-slavery-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slave-trade_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-slave-trade-87">[87]</a></sup> The majority of those enslaved and taken to these ports were captured in raids and wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Usually, the captives were taken back to the conquerors' territory as <a href="/wiki/Forced_labour" title="Forced labour">forced labour</a>; after time, they were sometimes acculturated and absorbed into the conquerors' society. Slave routes were established throughout Nigeria linking the hinterland areas with the major coastal ports. Some of the more prolific slave-trading kingdoms who participated in the transatlantic slave trade were linked with the Edo's <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Benin" title="Kingdom of Benin">Benin Empire</a> in the south, <a href="/wiki/Oyo_Empire" title="Oyo Empire">Oyo Empire</a> in the southwest, and the <a href="/wiki/Aro_Confederacy" title="Aro Confederacy">Aro Confederacy</a> in the southeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diverse-slavery_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-diverse-slavery-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slave-trade_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-slave-trade-87">[87]</a></sup> Benin's power lasted between the 15th and 19th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Oyo, at its territorial zenith in the late 17th to early 18th centuries, extended its influence from western Nigeria to modern-day <a href="/wiki/Togo" title="Togo">Togo</a>.
</p><p>In the north, the incessant fighting amongst the Hausa city-states and the decline of the <a href="/wiki/Kanem%E2%80%93Bornu_Empire" title="Kanem–Bornu Empire">Bornu Empire</a> gave rise to the Fulani people gaining headway into the region. Until this point, the Fulani a <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> ethnic group primarily traversed the semi-desert <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahelian</a> region, north of Sudan, with cattle and avoided trade and intermingling with the Sudanic peoples. At the beginning of the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Usman_dan_Fodio" title="Usman dan Fodio">Usman dan Fodio</a> led <a href="/wiki/Fulani_War" title="Fulani War">a successful jihad</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Hausa_Kingdoms" title="Hausa Kingdoms">Hausa Kingdoms</a> founding the centralised <a href="/wiki/Sokoto_Caliphate" title="Sokoto Caliphate">Sokoto Caliphate</a>. The empire with Arabic as its official language grew rapidly under his rule and that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Sultans_of_Sokoto" title="List of Sultans of Sokoto">his descendants</a>, who sent out <a href="/wiki/Fula_jihads" title="Fula jihads">invading armies</a> in every direction. The vast landlocked empire connected the east with the western Sudan region and made inroads down south conquering parts of the Oyo Empire (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Kwara_State" title="Kwara State">Kwara</a>), and advanced towards the <a href="/wiki/Yorubaland" title="Yorubaland">Yoruba heartland</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ibadan" title="Ibadan">Ibadan</a>, to reach the Atlantic Ocean. The territory controlled by the empire included much of modern-day northern and central Nigeria. The sultan sent out emirs to establish a <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerainty</a> over the conquered territories and promote Islamic civilization, the emirs in turn became increasingly rich and powerful through trade and slavery. By the 1890s, the largest <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Africa" title="Slavery in Africa">slave population</a> in the world, about two million, was concentrated in the territories of the Sokoto Caliphate. The use of slave labour was extensive, especially in agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> By the time of its break-up in 1903 into various European colonies, the Sokoto Caliphate was one of the largest pre-colonial African states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_colonization">British colonization</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Nigeria" title="Colonial Nigeria">Colonial Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emir_of_Kano-1911.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="469" data-file-width="760" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Emir_of_Kano-1911.jpg/220px-Emir_of_Kano-1911.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Emir_of_Kano-1911.jpg/330px-Emir_of_Kano-1911.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Emir_of_Kano-1911.jpg/440px-Emir_of_Kano-1911.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emir_of_Kano-1911.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Abbas_(Emir_of_Kano)" title="Muhammad Abbas (Emir of Kano)">Emir of Kano</a> with cavalry, 1911</div></div></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:King-duke.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="655" data-file-width="374" decoding="async" height="385" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8f/King-duke.jpg/220px-King-duke.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8f/King-duke.jpg/330px-King-duke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/8f/King-duke.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:King-duke.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Duke_IX" title="King Duke IX">King Duke IX</a> of <a href="/wiki/Calabar" title="Calabar">Calabar</a> in full dress (published 1895)</div></div></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stamp_Cameroons_2d-600px.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="395" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Stamp_Cameroons_2d-600px.jpg/220px-Stamp_Cameroons_2d-600px.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Stamp_Cameroons_2d-600px.jpg/330px-Stamp_Cameroons_2d-600px.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Stamp_Cameroons_2d-600px.jpg/440px-Stamp_Cameroons_2d-600px.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stamp_Cameroons_2d-600px.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1953 postage stamp with portrait of Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth<span class="nowrap"> </span>II</a></div></div></div><p>A changing legal imperative (the outlawing of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">transatlantic slave trade</a> in 1807) and economic imperative (a desire for political and social stability) led most European powers to support the widespread cultivation of agricultural products, such as the palm, for use in European industry. European companies engaged in the Atlantic slave trade until it was outlawed in 1807 by Britain, which up until that point had been the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade#Destinations_and_flags_of_carriers" title="Atlantic slave trade">second largest actor</a> practicing the slave trade. After that, the trade actually continued, as illegal smugglers purchased slaves along the coast by native slavers. Britain's <a href="/wiki/West_Africa_Squadron" title="West Africa Squadron">West Africa Squadron</a> sought to intercept the smugglers at sea. The rescued slaves were taken to <a href="/wiki/Freetown" title="Freetown">Freetown</a>, a colony in West Africa originally established by <a href="/wiki/John_Clarkson_(abolitionist)" title="John Clarkson (abolitionist)">Lieutenant John Clarkson</a> for the resettlement of slaves freed by Britain in North America after the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>.
</p><p>Britain intervened in the Lagos kingship power struggle by bombarding Lagos in 1851, deposing the slave-trade-friendly <a href="/wiki/Kosoko" title="Kosoko">Oba Kosoko</a>, helping to install the amenable Oba <a href="/wiki/Akitoye" title="Akitoye">Akitoye</a> and signing the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_Between_Great_Britain_and_Lagos,_1_January_1852" title="Treaty Between Great Britain and Lagos, 1 January 1852">Treaty between Great Britain and Lagos</a> on 1<span class="nowrap"> </span>January 1852. <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">Britain</a> annexed Lagos as a <a href="/wiki/Crown_colony" title="Crown colony">crown colony</a> in August 1861 with the <a href="/wiki/Lagos_Treaty_of_Cession" title="Lagos Treaty of Cession">Lagos Treaty of Cession</a>. British missionaries expanded their operations and travelled further inland. In 1864, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Ajayi_Crowther" title="Samuel Ajayi Crowther">Samuel Ajayi Crowther</a> became the first African <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Nigeria" title="Church of Nigeria">bishop of the Anglican Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1885, British claims to a West African <a href="/wiki/Sphere_of_influence" title="Sphere of influence">sphere of influence</a> received recognition from other European nations at the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Conference" title="Berlin Conference">Berlin Conference</a>. The following year, it chartered the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Niger_Company" title="Royal Niger Company">Royal Niger Company</a> under the leadership of Sir <a href="/wiki/George_Taubman_Goldie" title="George Taubman Goldie">George Taubman Goldie</a>. By the late 19th and early 20th centuries, the company had vastly succeeded in subjugating the independent southern kingdoms along the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a>, the British conquered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benin_Empire" title="Benin Empire">Benin</a> in 1897, and, in the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Aro_War" title="Anglo-Aro War">Anglo-Aro War</a> (1901–1902), defeated other opponents. The defeat of these states opened up the Niger area to British rule. In 1900, the company's territory came under the direct control of the British government and established the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Nigeria_Protectorate" title="Southern Nigeria Protectorate">Southern Nigeria Protectorate</a> as a British protectorate and part of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, the foremost world power at the time.
</p><p>By 1902, the British had begun plans to move north into the Sokoto Caliphate. British General Lord <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Lugard,_1st_Baron_Lugard" title="Frederick Lugard, 1st Baron Lugard">Frederick Lugard</a> was tasked by the <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Office" title="Colonial Office">Colonial Office</a> to implement the agenda. Lugard used rivalries between many of the emirs in the southern reach of the caliphate and the central Sokoto administration to prevent any defence as he worked towards the capital. As the British approached the city of <a href="/wiki/Sokoto" title="Sokoto">Sokoto</a>, Sultan <a href="/wiki/Muhammadu_Attahiru_I" title="Muhammadu Attahiru I">Muhammadu Attahiru I</a> organized a quick defence of the city and fought the advancing British-led forces. The British force quickly won, sending Attahiru I and thousands of followers on a <a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdist</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegira" title="Hegira">hijra</a></i>. In the northeast, the decline of the <a href="/wiki/Kanem%E2%80%93Bornu_Empire" title="Kanem–Bornu Empire">Bornu Empire</a> gave rise to the British-controlled <a href="/wiki/Borno_Emirate" title="Borno Emirate">Borno Emirate</a> which established <a href="/wiki/Abubakar_Garbai_of_Borno" title="Abubakar Garbai of Borno">Abubakar Garbai of Borno</a> as ruler.
</p><p>In 1903, the British victory in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kano_(1903)" title="Battle of Kano (1903)">Battle of Kano</a> gave them a logistical edge in <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kwatarkwashi" title="Battle of Kwatarkwashi">pacifying the heartland of the Sokoto Caliphate</a> and parts of the former Bornu Empire. On 13 March 1903, at the grand market square of Sokoto, the last <a href="/wiki/Sokoto_Grand_Vizier" title="Sokoto Grand Vizier">vizier of the caliphate</a> officially conceded to British rule. The British appointed <a href="/wiki/Muhammadu_Attahiru_II" title="Muhammadu Attahiru II">Muhammadu Attahiru II</a> as the new caliph. Lugard abolished the caliphate but retained the title <i>sultan</i> as a symbolic position in the newly organized <a href="/wiki/Northern_Nigeria_Protectorate" title="Northern Nigeria Protectorate">Northern Nigeria Protectorate</a>. This remnant became known as "<a href="/wiki/Sokoto_Sultanate_Council" title="Sokoto Sultanate Council">Sokoto Sultanate Council</a>". In June 1903, the British defeated the remaining forces of Attahiru. By 1906, resistance to British rule had ended.
</p><p>On 1 January 1914, the British formally united the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Nigeria_Protectorate" title="Southern Nigeria Protectorate">Southern Nigeria Protectorate</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Nigeria_Protectorate" title="Northern Nigeria Protectorate">Northern Nigeria Protectorate</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Nigeria" title="Colonial Nigeria">Colony and Protectorate of Nigeria</a>. Administratively, Nigeria remained divided into the Northern and Southern Protectorates and <a href="/wiki/Lagos_Colony" title="Lagos Colony">Lagos Colony</a>. Inhabitants of the southern region sustained more interaction, economic and cultural, with the British and other Europeans owing to the coastal economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Following <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, in response to the growth of Nigerian nationalism and demands for independence, successive constitutions legislated by the British government moved Nigeria toward self-government on a representative and increasingly federal basis. By the middle of the 20th century, a great <a href="/wiki/Decolonisation_of_Africa" title="Decolonisation of Africa">wave for independence</a> was sweeping across Africa.
</p><p>Christian missions established Western educational institutions in the protectorates. Under Britain's policy of indirect rule and validation of Islamic tradition, the Crown did not encourage the operation of Christian missions in the northern, Islamic part of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Some children of the southern elite went to Great Britain to pursue higher education. By independence in 1960, regional differences in modern educational access were marked. The legacy, though less pronounced, continues to the present day. Imbalances between north and south were expressed in Nigeria's political life as well. For instance, northern Nigeria did not outlaw slavery until 1936 whilst in other parts of Nigeria, slavery was abolished soon after colonialism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slave-trade_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-slave-trade-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nigeria gained a degree of self-rule in 1954, and full independence from the United Kingdom on 1 October 1960, as the <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Nigeria" title="Federation of Nigeria">Federation of Nigeria</a> with <a href="/wiki/Abubakar_Tafawa_Balewa" title="Abubakar Tafawa Balewa">Abubakar Tafawa Balewa</a> as its <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Nigeria" title="Prime Minister of Nigeria">prime minister</a>, while retaining the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Monarchy of the United Kingdom">British monarch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a>, as nominal head of state and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_of_Nigeria" title="Queen of Nigeria">Queen of Nigeria</a>. Azikiwe replaced the colonial <a href="/wiki/Governor-general" title="Governor-general">governor-general</a> in November 1960. At independence, the cultural and political differences were sharp among Nigeria's dominant ethnic groups: the <a href="/wiki/Hausa_people" title="Hausa people">Hausa</a> in the north, Igbo in the east and Yoruba in the west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBS2_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-JBS2-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>The founding government was a coalition of conservative parties: the <a href="/wiki/Northern_People%27s_Congress" title="Northern People's Congress">Northern People's Congress</a> led by Sir <a href="/wiki/Ahmadu_Bello" title="Ahmadu Bello">Ahmadu Bello</a>, a party dominated by Muslim northerners, and the Igbo and Christian-dominated <a href="/wiki/National_Council_of_Nigeria_and_the_Cameroons" title="National Council of Nigeria and the Cameroons">National Council of Nigeria and the Cameroons</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Nnamdi_Azikiwe" title="Nnamdi Azikiwe">Nnamdi Azikiwe</a>. The opposition comprised the comparatively liberal <a href="/wiki/Action_Group_(Nigeria)" title="Action Group (Nigeria)">Action Group</a>, which was largely dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_people" title="Yoruba people">Yoruba</a> and led by <a href="/wiki/Obafemi_Awolowo" title="Obafemi Awolowo">Obafemi Awolowo</a>. An imbalance was created in the polity as the result of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Cameroons#Trust_territory" title="Southern Cameroons">1961 plebiscite</a>. <a href="/wiki/Southern_Cameroons" title="Southern Cameroons">Southern Cameroons</a> opted to join the <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Republic of Cameroon</a> while <a href="/wiki/British_Cameroon" title="British Cameroon">Northern Cameroons</a> chose to join Nigeria. The northern part of the country became larger than the southern part.
</p>
<h3><span id="Independence.2C_First_Republic_.281960_-_1966.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence,_First_Republic_(1960_-_1966)">Independence, First Republic (1960 - 1966)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Timeline-Nigeria-en.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="698" data-file-width="1666" decoding="async" height="92" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Timeline-Nigeria-en.jpg/220px-Timeline-Nigeria-en.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Timeline-Nigeria-en.jpg/330px-Timeline-Nigeria-en.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Timeline-Nigeria-en.jpg/440px-Timeline-Nigeria-en.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Timeline-Nigeria-en.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Timeline after 1958; red: british rule, green: democracy, yellow: dictatorship, names: head of government (if at least 3 years of government)</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_Nigerian_Republic" title="First Nigerian Republic">First Nigerian Republic</a></div>
<p>The journey to independence saw the country attaining self-rule in some quarters in 1957 and total independence on 1 October 1960. Although <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Nigeria" title="Federation of Nigeria">Nigeria</a> gained independence from the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> on 1 October 1960, the nation retained the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_monarch" title="British monarch">British monarch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a>, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_of_Nigeria" title="Queen of Nigeria">titular head of state</a> until the adoption of a new constitution in 1963 declaring the nation a republic. The <a href="/wiki/Westminster_system" title="Westminster system">Westminster system</a> of government was retained, and thus the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Nigeria" title="President of Nigeria">President</a>'s powers were generally ceremonial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> The parliamentary system of government had<a href="/wiki/Abubakar_Tafawa_Balewa" title="Abubakar Tafawa Balewa">Abubakar Tafawa Balewa</a> as <a href="/wiki/Prime_minister" title="Prime minister">Prime Minister</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nnamdi_Azikiwe" title="Nnamdi Azikiwe">Nnamdi Azikiwe</a> as the ceremonial president. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coup_and_Counter-coup">Coup and Counter-coup</span></h3><p>
The disequilibrium and perceived corruption of the electoral and political process led to two <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">military coups</a> in 1966.
