The <a href="/wiki/1966_Nigerian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1966 Nigerian coup d'état">first coup was in January 1966</a> and was led mostly by soldiers under Majors <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Ifeajuna" title="Emmanuel Ifeajuna">Emmanuel Ifeajuna</a> (of the <a href="/wiki/Igbo_people" title="Igbo people">Igbo</a> tribe) and <a href="/wiki/Chukwuma_Kaduna_Nzeogwu" title="Chukwuma Kaduna Nzeogwu">Chukwuma Kaduna Nzeogwu</a> (<a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Northerner" title="Northerner">Northerner</a> of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Region,_Nigeria" title="Eastern Region, Nigeria">Eastern</a> extraction) and <a href="/wiki/Adewale_Ademoyega" title="Adewale Ademoyega">Adewale Ademoyega</a>. The coup plotters succeeded in assassinating Sir <a href="/wiki/Ahmadu_Bello" title="Ahmadu Bello">Ahmadu Bello</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abubakar_Tafawa_Balewa" title="Abubakar Tafawa Balewa">Abubakar Tafawa Balewa</a> alongside prominent leaders of the Northern Region and also Premier <a href="/wiki/Ladoke_Akintola" title="Ladoke Akintola">Samuel Akintola</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Western_State_(Nigeria)" title="Western State (Nigeria)">Western Region</a>, but the coup plotters struggled to form a central government. Senate President <a href="/wiki/Nwafor_Orizu" title="Nwafor Orizu">Nwafor Orizu</a> handed over government control to the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Army" title="Nigerian Army">Army</a>, under the command of another Igbo officer, General <a href="/wiki/Johnson_Aguiyi-Ironsi" title="Johnson Aguiyi-Ironsi">Johnson Aguiyi-Ironsi</a>. Later, the <a href="/wiki/1966_Nigerian_counter-coup" title="1966 Nigerian counter-coup">counter-coup of 1966</a>, supported primarily by Northern military officers, facilitated the rise of <a href="/wiki/Yakubu_Gowon" title="Yakubu Gowon">Yakubu Gowon</a> as military head of state. Tension rose between north and south; <a href="/wiki/1966_anti-Igbo_pogrom" title="1966 anti-Igbo pogrom">Igbos in northern cities suffered persecution</a> and many fled to the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Region,_Nigeria" title="Eastern Region, Nigeria">Eastern Region</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Biafra_independent_state_map-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Biafra_independent_state_map-en.svg/220px-Biafra_independent_state_map-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Biafra_independent_state_map-en.svg/330px-Biafra_independent_state_map-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Biafra_independent_state_map-en.svg/440px-Biafra_independent_state_map-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Biafra_independent_state_map-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Biafra" title="Biafra">Republic of Biafra</a> in June 1967, when it declared its independence from the rest of Nigeria</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_War">Civil War</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Civil_War" title="Nigerian Civil War">Nigerian Civil War</a></div>
<p>In May 1967, Governor of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Region,_Nigeria" title="Eastern Region, Nigeria">Eastern Region</a> Lt. Colonel <a href="/wiki/C._Odumegwu_Ojukwu" title="C. Odumegwu Ojukwu">Emeka Ojukwu</a> declared the region independent from the federation as a state called the <a href="/wiki/Biafra" title="Biafra">Republic of Biafra</a>, as a result of the continuous and systematically planned attacks against Igbos and those of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Region,_Nigeria" title="Eastern Region, Nigeria">Eastern</a> Extraction popularly known as 1966 <a href="/wiki/1966_anti-Igbo_pogrom" title="1966 anti-Igbo pogrom">pogroms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> This declaration precipitated the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Civil_War" title="Nigerian Civil War">Nigerian Civil War</a>, which began as the official Nigerian government side attacked Biafra on 6 July 1967, at Garkem. The 30-month war, with a long siege of Biafra and its isolation from trade and supplies, ended in January 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DDRS_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-DDRS-101">[101]</a></sup> Estimates of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_wars_by_death_toll" title="List of wars by death toll">number of dead</a> in the former Eastern Region during the 30-month civil war range from one to three million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> France, Egypt, the Soviet Union, Britain, Israel, and others were deeply involved in the civil war behind the scenes. Britain and the Soviet Union were the main military backers of the Nigerian government, with Nigeria utilizing air support from Egyptian pilots provided by <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> while France and Israel aided the Biafrans. The <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Congolese</a> government, under President <a href="/wiki/Mobutu_Sese_Seko" title="Mobutu Sese Seko">Joseph-Désiré Mobutu</a>, took an early stand on the Biafran secession, voicing strong support for the Nigerian federal government<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> and deploying thousands of troops to fight against the <a href="/wiki/Separatist_movements_of_Nigeria" title="Separatist movements of Nigeria">secessionists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Dictatorship_Gowon_.281966_-_1975.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Dictatorship_Gowon_(1966_-_1975)">Dictatorship Gowon (1966 - 1975)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship_in_Nigeria" title="Military dictatorship in Nigeria">Military dictatorship in Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yakubu_Gowon_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1076" data-file-width="807" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Yakubu_Gowon_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Yakubu_Gowon_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Yakubu_Gowon_%28cropped%29.jpg/256px-Yakubu_Gowon_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Yakubu_Gowon_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Yakubu_Gowon_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yakubu_Gowon_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Yakubu Gowon in 2007</div></div></div>
<p>Following the war, Nigeria enjoyed an <a href="/wiki/Oil_boom" title="Oil boom">oil boom</a> in the 1970s, during which the country joined <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a> and received huge oil revenues. Despite these revenues, the military government did little to improve the <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a> of the population, help small and medium businesses, or invest in infrastructure. As oil revenues fuelled the rise of federal subsidies to states, the federal government became the centre of political struggle and the threshold of power in the country. As oil production and revenue rose, the Nigerian government became increasingly dependent on oil revenues and international commodity markets for budgetary and economic concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coup_and_Counter-coup_once_more">Coup and Counter-coup once more</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1975_Nigerian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1975 Nigerian coup d'état">coup in July 1975</a>, led by Generals <a href="/wiki/Shehu_Musa_Yar%27Adua" title="Shehu Musa Yar'Adua">Shehu Musa Yar'Adua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Nanven_Garba" title="Joseph Nanven Garba">Joseph Garba</a>, ousted Gowon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIliffe201142–43Erfler201181_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIliffe201142–43Erfler201181-109">[109]</a></sup> who fled to Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEErfler201182_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEErfler201182-110">[110]</a></sup> The coup plotters wanted to replace Gowon's autocratic rule with a triumvirate of three brigadier generals whose decisions could be vetoed by a <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Military_Council_of_Nigeria_(1966%E2%80%931979)" title="Supreme Military Council of Nigeria (1966–1979)">Supreme Military Council</a>. For this triumvirate, they convinced General <a href="/wiki/Murtala_Muhammed" title="Murtala Muhammed">Murtala Muhammed</a> to become military head of state, with General <a href="/wiki/Olusegun_Obasanjo" title="Olusegun Obasanjo">Olusegun Obasanjo</a> as his second-in-command, and General <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Danjuma" title="Theophilus Danjuma">Theophilus Danjuma</a> as the third.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIliffe201143Erfler201181_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIliffe201143Erfler201181-111">[111]</a></sup> Together, the triumvirate introduced austerity measures to stem inflation, established a Corrupt Practices Investigation Bureau, replaced all military governors with new officers, and launched "Operation Deadwood" through which they fired 11,000 officials from the civil service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIliffe201144_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIliffe201144-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Colonel <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buka_Suka_Dimka" title="Buka Suka Dimka">Buka Suka Dimka</a> launched a <a href="/wiki/1976_Nigerian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt" title="1976 Nigerian coup d'état attempt">February 1976 coup attempt</a>, during which General Murtala Muhammed was assassinated. Dimka lacked widespread support among the military, and his coup failed, forcing him to flee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIliffe201148_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIliffe201148-113">[113]</a></sup> After the coup attempt, General Olusegun Obasanjo was appointed military head of state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIliffe201148–49Erfler201185_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIliffe201148–49Erfler201185-114">[114]</a></sup> As head of state, Obasanjo vowed to continue Murtala's policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIliffe201150Erfler201185_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIliffe201150Erfler201185-115">[115]</a></sup> Aware of the danger of alienating northern Nigerians, Obasanjo brought General Shehu Yar'Adua as his replacement and second-in-command as <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_Nigeria" title="Vice President of Nigeria">Chief of Staff, Supreme Headquarters</a> completing the military triumvirate, with Obasanjo as head of state and General <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Danjuma" title="Theophilus Danjuma">Theophilus Danjuma</a> as <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Army_Staff_(Nigeria)" title="Chief of Army Staff (Nigeria)">Chief of Army Staff</a>, the three went on to re-establish control over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nigerian_military_juntas_of_1966%E2%80%9379_and_1983%E2%80%9399" title="Nigerian military juntas of 1966–79 and 1983–99">military regime</a> and organized the military's transfer of power programme: <a href="/wiki/States_of_Nigeria" title="States of Nigeria">states creation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boundary_delimitation" title="Boundary delimitation">national delimitation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Local_government_areas_of_Nigeria" title="Local government areas of Nigeria">local government reforms</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Nigeria#1979_constitution_(Second_Republic)" title="Constitution of Nigeria">constitutional drafting committee</a> for a new republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="The_Second_Republic_.281979_-_1983.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Second_Republic_(1979_-_1983)">The Second Republic (1979 - 1983)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_Nigerian_Republic" title="Second Nigerian Republic">Second Nigerian Republic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shehu_Shagari_1980-10-07.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Shehu_Shagari_1980-10-07.jpg/170px-Shehu_Shagari_1980-10-07.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Shehu_Shagari_1980-10-07.jpg/255px-Shehu_Shagari_1980-10-07.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Shehu_Shagari_1980-10-07.jpg/339px-Shehu_Shagari_1980-10-07.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shehu_Shagari_1980-10-07.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shehu Shagari was the first democratically elected <a href="/wiki/President_of_Nigeria" title="President of Nigeria">President of Nigeria</a> from 1979 to 1983.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1977, a <a href="/wiki/Constituent_assembly" title="Constituent assembly">constituent assembly</a> was elected to draft a new constitution, which was published on September 21, 1978, when the ban on political activity was lifted. The military carefully planned the return to civilian rule putting in place measures to ensure that political parties had broader support than witnessed during the first republic. In 1979, five political parties competed in a series of elections in which Alhaji <a href="/wiki/Shehu_Shagari" title="Shehu Shagari">Shehu Shagari</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Party_of_Nigeria" title="National Party of Nigeria">National Party of Nigeria</a> (NPN) was elected president. All five parties won representation in the National Assembly. On October 1, 1979, Shehu Shagari was sworn in as the first <a href="/wiki/President_of_Nigeria" title="President of Nigeria">President and Commander-in-Chief</a> of the Federal Republic of Nigeria. Obasanjo peacefully transferred power to Shagari, becoming the first head of state in Nigerian history to willingly step down.
</p><p>The Shagari government became viewed as corrupt by virtually all sectors of Nigerian society. In 1983, the inspectors of the state-owned Nigerian National Petroleum Corporation began to notice "the slow poisoning of the waters of this country".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-igbo2_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-igbo2-118">[118]</a></sup> In August 1983 Shagari and the NPN were returned to power in a landslide victory, with a majority of seats in the National Assembly and control of 12 state governments. But the elections were marred by violence, and allegations of widespread vote-rigging and electoral malfeasance led to legal battles over the results. There were also uncertainties, such as in the first republic, that political leaders may be unable to govern properly.
</p>
<h3><span id="Coup_1983.2C_Dictatorship_Buhari.2C_Coup_1985_and_Dictatorship_Babangida_.281985_-_1992.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Coup_1983,_Dictatorship_Buhari,_Coup_1985_and_Dictatorship_Babangida_(1985_-_1992)">Coup 1983, Dictatorship Buhari, Coup 1985 and Dictatorship Babangida (1985 - 1992)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship_in_Nigeria" title="Military dictatorship in Nigeria">Military dictatorship in Nigeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Third_Nigerian_Republic" title="Third Nigerian Republic">Third Nigerian Republic</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1983_Nigerian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1983 Nigerian coup d'état">1983 military coup d'état</a> took place on New Year's Eve of that year. It was coordinated by key officers of the Nigerian military and led to the overthrow of the government and the installation of Major General <a href="/wiki/Muhammadu_Buhari" title="Muhammadu Buhari">Muhammadu Buhari</a> as head of state. The military coup of Muhammadu Buhari shortly after the regime's re-election in 1984 was generally viewed as a positive development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Buhari promised major reforms, but his government fared little better than its predecessor. General Buhari was overthrown in a <a href="/wiki/1985_Nigerian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1985 Nigerian coup d'état">1985 military coup d'état</a> led by General <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Babangida" title="Ibrahim Babangida">Ibrahim Babangida</a>, who established the <a href="/wiki/Armed_Forces_Ruling_Council_(Nigeria)" title="Armed Forces Ruling Council (Nigeria)">Armed Forces Ruling Council</a> and became military president and commander in chief of the armed forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Globe22_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Globe22-120">[120]</a></sup> In 1986, he established the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Political_Bureau_of_1986" title="Nigerian Political Bureau of 1986">Nigerian Political Bureau</a> which made recommendations for the transition to the Third Nigerian Republic. In 1989, Babangida started making plans for the transition to the Third Nigerian Republic. Babangida survived the <a href="/wiki/1990_Nigerian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt" title="1990 Nigerian coup d'état attempt">1990 Nigerian coup d'état attempt</a>, then postponed a promised return to democracy to 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="The_short-lived_Third_Republic_.281992_-_1993.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_short-lived_Third_Republic_(1992_-_1993)">The short-lived Third Republic (1992 - 1993)</span></h3>
<p>He legalized the formation of political parties and formed the two-party system with the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_(Nigeria)" title="Social Democratic Party (Nigeria)">Social Democratic Party</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Convention" title="National Republican Convention">National Republican Convention</a> ahead of the <a href="/wiki/1992_Nigerian_parliamentary_election" title="1992 Nigerian parliamentary election">1992 general elections</a>. He urged all Nigerians to join either of the parties, which Chief <a href="/wiki/Bola_Ige" title="Bola Ige">Bola Ige</a> referred to as "two leper hands." The two-party state had been a Political Bureau recommendation. After a census was conducted, the <a href="/wiki/Independent_National_Electoral_Commission" title="Independent National Electoral Commission">National Electoral Commission</a> announced on 24 January 1992, that both <a href="/wiki/1992_Nigerian_parliamentary_election" title="1992 Nigerian parliamentary election">legislative elections</a> to a bicameral <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Nigeria)" title="National Assembly (Nigeria)">National Assembly</a> and a presidential election would be held later that year. The adopted process advocated that any candidate needed to pass through adoption for all elective positions from the local government, state government and federal government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP2_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP2-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1993_Nigerian_presidential_election" title="1993 Nigerian presidential election">1993 presidential election</a> held on 12 June, was the first since the military coup of 1983. The results, though not officially declared by the National Electoral Commission, showed the duo of <a href="/wiki/Moshood_Abiola" title="Moshood Abiola">Moshood Abiola</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baba_Gana_Kingibe" title="Baba Gana Kingibe">Baba Gana Kingibe</a> of the Social Democratic Party defeated <a href="/wiki/Bashir_Tofa" title="Bashir Tofa">Bashir Tofa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sylvester_Ugoh" title="Sylvester Ugoh">Sylvester Ugoh</a> of the National Republican Convention by over 2.3 million votes. However, Babangida annulled the elections, leading to massive civilian protests that effectively shut down the country for weeks. In August 1993, Babangida finally kept his promise to relinquish power to a civilian government but not before appointing <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Shonekan" title="Ernest Shonekan">Ernest Shonekan</a> head of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Ernest_Shonekan" title="Cabinet of Ernest Shonekan">interim national government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Babangida's regime has been considered the most corrupt and responsible for creating a culture of corruption in Nigeria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Coup_1993_and_Dictatorship_Abacha_.281993_-_1998.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Coup_1993_and_Dictatorship_Abacha_(1993_-_1998)">Coup 1993 and Dictatorship Abacha (1993 - 1998)</span></h3>
<p>Shonekan's interim government, the shortest in the political history of the country, was overthrown in a <a href="/wiki/1993_Nigerian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1993 Nigerian coup d'état">coup d'état of 1993</a> led by General <a href="/wiki/Sani_Abacha" title="Sani Abacha">Sani Abacha</a>, who used military force on a wide scale to suppress the continuing civilian unrest.
</p><p>In 1995, the government hanged environmentalist Ken Saro-Wiwa on trumped-up charges in the deaths of four <a href="/wiki/Ogoni_people" title="Ogoni people">Ogoni</a> elders. Which caused Nigerian's <a href="/wiki/Suspension_from_the_Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Suspension from the Commonwealth of Nations">suspension from Commonwealth</a>. Lawsuits under the American <a href="/wiki/Alien_Tort_Statute" title="Alien Tort Statute">Alien Tort Statute</a> against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell" title="Royal Dutch Shell">Royal Dutch Shell</a> and Brian Anderson, the head of Shell's Nigerian operation, settled out of court with Shell continuing to deny liability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Several hundred million dollars in accounts traced to Abacha were discovered in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The regime came to an end in 1998 when the dictator died in the villa. He looted money to offshore accounts in western European banks and defeated coup plots by arresting and bribing generals and politicians. His successor, General <a href="/wiki/Abdulsalami_Abubakar" title="Abdulsalami Abubakar">Abdulsalami Abubakar</a>, adopted a new constitution on May 5, 1999, which provided for multiparty elections.
</p>
<h3><span id="Fourth_Republic_.281999.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fourth_Republic_(1999–present)">Fourth Republic (1999–present)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Nigerian_Republic" title="Fourth Nigerian Republic">Fourth Nigerian Republic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Olusegun_Obasanjo_DD-SC-07-14396-cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="1198" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Olusegun_Obasanjo_DD-SC-07-14396-cropped.jpg/170px-Olusegun_Obasanjo_DD-SC-07-14396-cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Olusegun_Obasanjo_DD-SC-07-14396-cropped.jpg/255px-Olusegun_Obasanjo_DD-SC-07-14396-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Olusegun_Obasanjo_DD-SC-07-14396-cropped.jpg/340px-Olusegun_Obasanjo_DD-SC-07-14396-cropped.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Olusegun_Obasanjo_DD-SC-07-14396-cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Olusegun_Obasanjo" title="Olusegun Obasanjo">Olusegun Obasanjo</a> was civilian President of Nigeria from 1999 to 2007.</div></div></div>
<p>Dictator Abacha died in 1998 under undignified circumstances at a "party".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> His successor, Abdulsalami Abubakar, adopted a constitution on 5 May 1999 that had been drafted ten years earlier and provided for free elections with several parties.
</p>
<h4><span id="The_Obasanjo_Era_.281999_to_2007.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Obasanjo_Era_(1999_to_2007)">The Obasanjo Era (1999 to 2007)</span></h4>
<p>On 29 May 1999, Abubakar handed over power to the winner of the 1999 presidential election, former military ruler General Olusegun Obasanjo, as President of Nigeria. Obasanjo had been in prison under dictator Abacha. Obasanjo's election heralded the beginning of the Fourth Nigerian Republic, which lasts until today (2022).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> This ended a 39-year period of short-lived democracies, military coups and counter-coups.
</p><p><i>First term</i>
</p><p>During his first term, Nigerians' freedoms increased; freedom of the press allowed criticism of the president for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the first months of his presidency, Obasanjo retired about 200 military officers, including all 93 who held political office, making a coup by experienced officers less likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-129">[129]</a></sup> He also moved the Ministry of Defence from <a href="/wiki/Lagos" title="Lagos">Lagos</a> to <a href="/wiki/Abuja" title="Abuja">Abuja</a> and ensured that it was placed under more direct government control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Obasanjo was re-elected in 2003 in a turbulent election with violent ethnic and religious undertones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Second term</i>
</p><p>On 12 June 2006, he signed the Greentree Agreement with Cameroonian President <a href="/wiki/Paul_Biya" title="Paul Biya">Paul Biya</a>, formally ending the border dispute over the <a href="/wiki/Bakassi" title="Bakassi">Bakassi Peninsula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Even when the Nigerian Senate passed a resolution declaring the withdrawal of Nigerian troops from the Bakassi Peninsula illegal, Obasanjo gave orders for it to continue as planned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his second term, Obasanjo ensured the expansion of the Nigerian police.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_129-3"><a href="#cite_note-:4-129">[129]</a></sup> Ethnic violence over control of the oil-producing Niger Delta region and an insurgency in the northeast continued. Conflicts between Muslims and Christians in <a href="/wiki/Plateau_State" title="Plateau State">Plateau State</a> prompted Obasanjo to declare a state of emergency in May 2004, suspend the state government and install a six-month military government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_129-4"><a href="#cite_note-:4-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Plans for third term</i>
</p><p>Obasanjo tried to amend the constitution to allow him to run for a third term - a process that often heralds the transition to another dictatorship in other African countries in the region. This led to tensions with parliament and with the vice president. An autobiography by <a href="/wiki/Condoleezza_Rice" title="Condoleezza Rice">Condoleeza Rice</a> claims that then US President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> ultimately convinced Obasanjo to abandon these plans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Conclusion</i>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:YarAdua_WEF_2008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="730" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/YarAdua_WEF_2008.jpg/170px-YarAdua_WEF_2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/YarAdua_WEF_2008.jpg/255px-YarAdua_WEF_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/YarAdua_WEF_2008.jpg/340px-YarAdua_WEF_2008.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:YarAdua_WEF_2008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Yar'Adua, president 2007 - 2010</div></div></div>
<p>Although the elections that brought Obasanjo to power and allowed him to run for a second term in the 2003 presidential elections were condemned as unfree and unfair, Nigeria made significant progress in democratisation under Obasanjo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> The fact that parliament was able to successfully deny the president a third term in office, despite his influence on the army and security forces, is evidence of the strengthened parliamentarism in Nigeria after 2000. Obasanjo was also able to largely repair the diplomatic damage of the Abacha dictatorship through an active foreign policy (for example, by being readmitted to the Commonwealth).
</p>
<h4><span id="The_Yar.27Adua_era_.282007_to_2010.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Yar'Adua_era_(2007_to_2010)">The Yar'Adua era (2007 to 2010)</span></h4>
<p>In the 2007 general elections, <a href="/wiki/Umaru_Musa_Yar%27Adua" title="Umaru Musa Yar'Adua">Umaru Yar'Adua</a> of the People's Democratic Party came to power. The international community, which had observed the Nigerian elections to promote a free and fair process, condemned these elections as seriously flawed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Outgoing President Olusegun Obasanjo acknowledged fraud and other "shortcomings" in the elections, but said the result was in line with opinion polls. In a nationally televised address in 2007, he added that if Nigerians did not like the victory of his handpicked successor, they had the option of voting again in four years' time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Yar'Adua's health would soon make this option pointless.
</p>
<h4><span id="The_Jonathan_Era_.282010_to_2015.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Jonathan_Era_(2010_to_2015)">The Jonathan Era (2010 to 2015)</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goodluck_Jonathan_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="613" data-file-width="515" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Goodluck_Jonathan_2014.jpg/170px-Goodluck_Jonathan_2014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Goodluck_Jonathan_2014.jpg/254px-Goodluck_Jonathan_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Goodluck_Jonathan_2014.jpg/339px-Goodluck_Jonathan_2014.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goodluck_Jonathan_2014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Goodluck Jonathan, president 2010 - 2015</div></div></div>
<p>Yar'Adua died on 5 May 2010, and Vice President <a href="/wiki/Goodluck_Jonathan" title="Goodluck Jonathan">Goodluck Jonathan</a> had been sworn in 3 months earlier to succeed Yar'Adua,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> who was seriously ill and receiving treatment abroad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Jonathan won the 2011 presidential election and the international media reported that, unlike previous elections, the polls went smoothly and with relatively little violence or electoral fraud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_York2_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-New_York2-142">[142]</a></sup> Jonathan's tenure includes the successful fight against Ebola and an economic recovery that made Nigeria the leading economic power in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> The Jonathan administration's film promotion of high-quality productions created Nigerias own commercially successful <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Nigeria" title="Cinema of Nigeria">film industry ("Nollywood")</a>, which can only be compared to the <a href="/wiki/Hollywood,_Los_Angeles" title="Hollywood, Los Angeles">USA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hindi_cinema" title="Hindi cinema">India ("Bollywood")</a>. On the debit side of the ledger, on the other hand, falls the wave of terror by <a href="/wiki/Boko_Haram_insurgency" title="Boko Haram insurgency">Boko Haram</a>, which kidnapped 200 schoolgirls in <a href="/wiki/Chibok_schoolgirls_kidnapping" title="Chibok schoolgirls kidnapping">Chibok</a> in 2014 and highlighted the impotence of the Nigerian state (about 100 of the girls are still missing in 2022). Above all, however, Jonathan's tenure stands for the misappropriation of state funds. 20 billion US dollars are said to have been lost to the Nigerian state as a result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The high level of corruption was a determining factor in the 2015 presidential election, in which "clean man" Muhammadu Buhari replaced incumbent Jonathan, who was running for re-election. This is (as of 2022) the only instance in the IV. Republic where Nigerian voters refused to re-elect an incumbent president. Jonathans party, the PDP, lost power after 16 years of government. At least it can be said of Jonathan that he conceded defeat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="The_Buhari_Era_.282015_to_today.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Buhari_Era_(2015_to_today)">The Buhari Era (2015 to today)</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muhammadu_Buhari,_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5760" data-file-width="4800" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Muhammadu_Buhari%2C_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Muhammadu_Buhari%2C_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Muhammadu_Buhari%2C_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Muhammadu_Buhari%2C_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Muhammadu_Buhari%2C_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Muhammadu_Buhari%2C_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Muhammadu_Buhari,_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Muhammadu_Buhari" title="Muhammadu Buhari">Muhammadu Buhari</a> is currently serving as President of Nigeria, since 2015.</div></div></div>
<p>Ahead of the <a href="/wiki/2015_Nigerian_general_election" title="2015 Nigerian general election">general election of 2015</a>, a merger of the biggest opposition parties – the <a href="/wiki/Action_Congress_of_Nigeria" title="Action Congress of Nigeria">Action Congress of Nigeria</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Congress_for_Progressive_Change" title="Congress for Progressive Change">Congress for Progressive Change</a>, the <a href="/wiki/All_Nigeria_Peoples_Party" title="All Nigeria Peoples Party">All Nigeria Peoples Party</a> (a faction of the <a href="/wiki/All_Progressives_Grand_Alliance" title="All Progressives Grand Alliance">All Progressives Grand Alliance</a>), and the new PDP (a faction of serving governors of the ruling People's Democratic Party) – formed the <a href="/wiki/All_Progressives_Congress" title="All Progressives Congress">All Progressives Congress</a>. Their candidate, former dictator Muhammadu Buhari – who had previously contested in the 2003, 2007, and 2011 presidential elections—won by over two million votes. Observers generally praised the election as being fair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/2019_Nigerian_general_election" title="2019 Nigerian general election">2019 presidential election</a>, Buhari was re-elected for a second term in office defeating his closet rival <a href="/wiki/Atiku_Abubakar" title="Atiku Abubakar">Atiku Abubakar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a Nigerian response to the US <a href="/wiki/Black_Lives_Matter" title="Black Lives Matter">Black Lives Matter</a> movement, in 2020 mainly young Nigerians protested against police attacks. The Special Anti-Robbery Squad (SARS) was particularly criticised. The government disbanded the unit and promised to improve conditions. However, during a demonstration in October 2020, security forces shot dozens of people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> The security situation eased slightly in 2022, when Boko Haram, whose militias terrorised Nigeria's north for years and devastated entire regions, largely disbanded. 40,000 Boko Haram fighters surrendered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Buhari administration in 2022 can take credit for having renovated and built new roads, <a href="/wiki/Lekki_Port" title="Lekki Port">ports</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second_Niger_bridge" title="Second Niger bridge">bridges</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Railway_Corporation" title="Nigerian Railway Corporation">railways</a> like no previous government. In the case of national resources such as oil and rice, it has successfully attracted or established processing industries (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Dangote_Refinery" title="Dangote Refinery">Dangote refinery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imota_rice_mill" title="Imota rice mill">Imota rice mill</a>). Major Nigerian entrepreneurs such as <a href="/wiki/Aliko_Dangote" title="Aliko Dangote">Aliko Dangote</a> (cement), <a href="/wiki/Innocent_Chukwuma" title="Innocent Chukwuma">Innocent Chukwuma</a> (<a href="/wiki/Innoson_Vehicle_Manufacturing" title="Innoson Vehicle Manufacturing">Innoson</a>, vehicle manufacturing) and <a href="/wiki/Stella_Chinyelu_Okoli" title="Stella Chinyelu Okoli">Dr. Stella C. Okoli</a> (Emzor, pharmaceuticals) no longer take their wealth abroad, but increasingly invest it at home.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_election_2023">Presidential election 2023</span></h4>
<p>For the first time in the IV Republic, three promising candidates will face each other in the February 2023 presidential election: <a href="/wiki/Bola_Tinubu" title="Bola Tinubu">Bola Tinubu</a> of the neoliberal ruling party All Progressives, <a href="/wiki/Atiku_Abubakar" title="Atiku Abubakar">Atiku Abubakar</a> of the People's Democratic Party and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Obi" title="Peter Obi">Peter Obi</a> of the Labour Party. Moreover, for the first time in the IV. Republic, no officer or former military ruler is running for president. Peter Obi, previously the successful governor of <a href="/wiki/Anambra_State" title="Anambra State">Anambra State</a>, is well ahead in opinion polls (as of October 2022).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> He appeals mainly to young, urban voters and has his core base in the Southeast. After the aged and sickly President Buhari, the 51-year-old Obi may appear as a pleasantly young alternative next to his two opposing candidates who are over 70 years old.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Politics_of_Nigeria" title="Politics of Nigeria">Politics of Nigeria</a></div>
<p>Nigeria is a <a href="/wiki/Federal_republic" title="Federal republic">federal republic</a> modelled after the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> with executive power exercised by the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Nigeria" title="President of Nigeria">President</a>. The president is both <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of the federal government</a>; the president is <a href="/wiki/Direct_election" title="Direct election">elected by popular vote</a> to a maximum of two four-year terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factbook3_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-factbook3-158">[158]</a></sup> The president's power is checked by a <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Nigeria" title="Senate of Nigeria">Senate</a> and a <a href="/wiki/House_of_Representatives_(Nigeria)" title="House of Representatives (Nigeria)">House of Representatives</a>, which are combined in a <a href="/wiki/Bicameralism" title="Bicameralism">bicameral body</a> called the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Nigeria)" title="National Assembly (Nigeria)">National Assembly</a>. The Senate is a 109-seat body with three members from each state and one from the capital region of Abuja; members are elected by popular vote to four-year terms. The House contains 360 seats, with the number of seats per state determined by population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factbook3_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-factbook3-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:National_Assembly_Building,_Abuja,_Nigeria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3840" data-file-width="5760" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/National_Assembly_Building%2C_Abuja%2C_Nigeria.jpg/220px-National_Assembly_Building%2C_Abuja%2C_Nigeria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/National_Assembly_Building%2C_Abuja%2C_Nigeria.jpg/330px-National_Assembly_Building%2C_Abuja%2C_Nigeria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/National_Assembly_Building%2C_Abuja%2C_Nigeria.jpg/440px-National_Assembly_Building%2C_Abuja%2C_Nigeria.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:National_Assembly_Building,_Abuja,_Nigeria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Nigeria)" title="National Assembly (Nigeria)">Nigerian National Assembly</a>, Abuja</div></div></div>
<p>Ethnocentrism, tribalism, religious persecution, and <a href="/wiki/Prebendalism" title="Prebendalism">prebendalism</a> have plagued Nigerian politics both before and after independence in 1960. All major parties have practised vote-rigging and other means of coercion to remain competitive. In the period before 1983 election, a report prepared by the National Institute of Policy and Strategic Studies showed that only the 1959 and 1979 elections were held without systemic rigging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> In 2012, Nigeria was estimated to have lost over $400 billion to <a href="/wiki/Corruption_in_Nigeria" title="Corruption in Nigeria">corruption since independence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Kin-selective altruism is prevalent in Nigerian politics, resulting in tribalist efforts to concentrate Federal power to a particular region of their interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> Because of the above issues, Nigeria's political parties are <a href="/wiki/Pan-nationalism" title="Pan-nationalism">pan-national</a> and secular in character (though this does not preclude the continuing preeminence of the dominant ethnicities).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rashid32_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-rashid32-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> The two major political parties are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Democratic_Party_(Nigeria)" title="People's Democratic Party (Nigeria)">People's Democratic Party of Nigeria</a> and the <a href="/wiki/All_Progressives_Congress" title="All Progressives Congress">All Progressives Congress</a>, with twenty registered minor opposition parties.
</p><p>Hausa-Fulani, Yoruba and Igbo are the three largest ethnic groups in Nigeria and have maintained historical preeminence in Nigerian politics; competition amongst these three groups has fuelled animosity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rashid32_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-rashid32-162">[162]</a></sup> Following the bloody civil war, nationalism has seen an increase in the southern part of the country leading to active secessionist movements such as the <a href="/wiki/Oodua_Peoples_Congress" title="Oodua Peoples Congress">Oodua Peoples Congress</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Movement_for_the_Actualization_of_the_Sovereign_State_of_Biafra" title="Movement for the Actualization of the Sovereign State of Biafra">Movement for the Actualization of the Sovereign State of Biafra</a>, though these groups are generally small they are somewhat representative of the entire ethnic group.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Nigeria" title="Law of Nigeria">Law of Nigeria</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Nigeria" title="Law of Nigeria">Law of Nigeria</a>  consists of courts, offences, and various types of laws. Nigeria has its own <a href="/wiki/Constitution" title="Constitution">constitution</a> which was established on 29 May 1999. The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Nigeria" title="Constitution of Nigeria">Constitution of Nigeria</a> is the supreme law of the country. There are four distinct legal systems in Nigeria, which include <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">English law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">Common law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Customary_law" title="Customary law">Customary law</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia"> Sharia Law</a>. English law in Nigeria is derived from the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">colonial</a> Nigeria, while common law is a development from its post colonial independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>Customary law is derived from indigenous traditional norms and practices, including the dispute resolution meetings of pre-colonial Yoruba land secret societies and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egbo" title="Egbo">Èkpè</a> and Okónkò of <a href="/wiki/Igboland" title="Igboland">Igboland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ibibio_people" title="Ibibio people">Ibibioland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia Law</a> <i>(also known as Islamic Law)</i> used to be used only in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Nigeria" title="Northern Nigeria">Northern Nigeria</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> is the predominant <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>. It is also being used in <a href="/wiki/Lagos_State" title="Lagos State">Lagos State</a>, Oyo State, Kwara State, Ogun State, and Osun State by Muslims. The country has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judicial_branch" title="Judicial branch">judicial branch</a>, the highest court of which is the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Nigeria" title="Supreme Court of Nigeria">Supreme Court of Nigeria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Armed_Forces" title="Nigerian Armed Forces">Nigerian Armed Forces</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Nigerian_Armed_Forces.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Flag_of_the_Nigerian_Armed_Forces.svg/220px-Flag_of_the_Nigerian_Armed_Forces.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Flag_of_the_Nigerian_Armed_Forces.svg/330px-Flag_of_the_Nigerian_Armed_Forces.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Flag_of_the_Nigerian_Armed_Forces.svg/440px-Flag_of_the_Nigerian_Armed_Forces.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Nigerian_Armed_Forces.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flag of the Nigerian Armed Forces</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Armed_Forces" title="Nigerian Armed Forces">Nigerian Armed Forces</a> are the <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">combined military forces</a> of Nigeria. It consists of three uniformed service branches: the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Army" title="Nigerian Army">Nigerian Army</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Navy" title="Nigerian Navy">Nigerian Navy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Air_Force" title="Nigerian Air Force">Nigerian Air Force</a>. The <a href="/wiki/President_of_Nigeria" title="President of Nigeria">President of Nigeria</a> functions as the <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">commander-in-chief</a> of the armed forces, exercising his <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Nigeria" title="Constitution of Nigeria">constitutional authority</a> through the Ministry of Defence, which is responsible for the management of the military and its personnel. The operational head of the AFN is the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_the_Defence_Staff_(Nigeria)" title="Chief of the Defence Staff (Nigeria)">Chief of the Defence Staff</a>, who is subordinate to the <a href="/wiki/Defence_Minister_of_Nigeria" title="Defence Minister of Nigeria">Nigerian Defence Minister</a>. With a force of more than 223,000 active personnel, the Nigerian military is one of the largest uniformed combat services in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> According to Global Firepower, the Nigerian Armed Forces are the fourth-most powerful military in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, and ranked 35th on its list internationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Nigerian Armed Forces were established in 1960 as the successor to the combat units of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_West_African_Frontier_Force" title="Royal West African Frontier Force">Royal West African Frontier Force</a> stationed in the country, which had previously served as the British Empire's multi-battalion <a href="/wiki/Field_force" title="Field force">field force</a> during Nigeria's <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Nigeria" title="Colonial Nigeria">protectorate period</a>. Shortly after its formation, the NAF was engaged in combat operations against the secessionist state of <a href="/wiki/Biafra" title="Biafra">Biafra</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Civil_War" title="Nigerian Civil War">Nigerian Civil War</a> from 1967 to 1970. At this point in time, the Nigerian military ballooned in strength from 85,000 personnel in 1967, to more than 250,000 troops by the war's end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-REF00_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-REF00-169">[169]</a></sup> In the years following the civil war, the Nigerian Armed Forces were halved in size from its post-war height to approximately 125,000 men. In spite of this contraction in the size and funding of its Armed Forces, Nigeria would boast the only military in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> capable of engaging in foreign military operations, such as during its intervention in <a href="/wiki/First_Liberian_Civil_War" title="First Liberian Civil War">Liberian civil war</a> in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> Nigeria's Armed Forces would continue to remain an active element in combat operations throughout the African continent over the proceeding decades, with notable engagements including its 2017 involvement as part of the <a href="/wiki/Economic_Community_of_West_African_States" title="Economic Community of West African States">ECOWAS</a> <a href="/wiki/ECOWAS_military_intervention_in_the_Gambia" title="ECOWAS military intervention in the Gambia">military intervention</a> in <a href="/wiki/The_Gambia" title="The Gambia">the Gambia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ZSU-23-4_Shilka_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1426" data-file-width="2248" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/ZSU-23-4_Shilka_01.jpg/220px-ZSU-23-4_Shilka_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/ZSU-23-4_Shilka_01.jpg/330px-ZSU-23-4_Shilka_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/ZSU-23-4_Shilka_01.jpg/440px-ZSU-23-4_Shilka_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ZSU-23-4_Shilka_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Army" title="Nigerian Army">Nigerian Army</a> self-propelled anti-aircraft gun</div></div></div>
<p>Today, the NAF faces a number of domestic challenges which continue to undermine stability within Nigeria and the region as a whole. Some of these threats include the ongoing conflict against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jihadist" title="Jihadist">jihadist</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_rebel_groups_that_control_territory" title="List of rebel groups that control territory">rebel group</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boko_Haram" title="Boko Haram">Boko Haram</a> in <a href="/wiki/Boko_Haram_insurgency" title="Boko Haram insurgency">northeastern Nigeria</a>, which has been in effect since July 2009. Likewise, Nigeria has been engaged in a long-running <a href="/wiki/Conflict_in_the_Niger_Delta" title="Conflict in the Niger Delta">anti-piracy campaign</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Niger_Delta" title="Niger Delta">Niger Delta</a>, which has threatened the vital <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry_in_Nigeria" title="Petroleum industry in Nigeria">petroleum industry</a> in the country, which is the source of 40% of Nigeria's exports and 85% of the government's revenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reuters_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Compounding this state of affairs is the role corruption plays in the ongoing attempts to strengthen the armed forces. Corruption has historically weakened the Nigerian military's capacity to face internal security threats, and is cited as being responsible for the continued longevity of rebels and terrorists operating throughout the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Nigeria" title="Foreign relations of Nigeria">Foreign relations of Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MinForeignAffairs.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1520" data-file-width="2688" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/MinForeignAffairs.jpg/220px-MinForeignAffairs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/MinForeignAffairs.jpg/330px-MinForeignAffairs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/MinForeignAffairs.jpg/440px-MinForeignAffairs.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MinForeignAffairs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Ministry of <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Affairs" title="Foreign Affairs">Foreign Affairs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abuja" title="Abuja">Abuja</a></div></div></div>
<p>Upon gaining independence in 1960, Nigeria made African unity the centrepiece of its foreign policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> One exception to the African focus was Nigeria's close relationship developed with Israel throughout the 1960s. Israel sponsored and oversaw the construction of Nigeria's parliament buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nigeria's foreign policy was put to the test in the 1970s after the country emerged united from its civil war. It supported movements against white minority governments in the Southern Africa sub-region. Nigeria backed the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">African National Congress</a> by taking a committed tough line about the South African government. Nigeria was a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_African_Unity" title="Organisation of African Unity">Organisation for African Unity</a> (now the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a>) and has tremendous influence in West Africa and Africa on the whole. Nigeria founded regional cooperative efforts in West Africa, functioning as the standard-bearer for the <a href="/wiki/Economic_Community_of_West_African_States" title="Economic Community of West African States">Economic Community of West African States</a> (ECOWAS) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ECOMOG" title="ECOMOG">ECOMOG</a> (especially during the Liberia and Sierra Leone civil wars) - which are economic and military organizations, respectively.
</p><p>With this Africa-centred stance, Nigeria readily <a href="/wiki/Congo_Crisis" title="Congo Crisis">sent troops to the Congo</a> at the behest of the United Nations shortly after independence (and has maintained membership since that time). Nigeria also supported several Pan-African and pro-self government causes in the 1970s, including garnering support for <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Popular_Movement_for_the_Liberation_of_Angola" title="Popular Movement for the Liberation of Angola">MPLA</a>, <a href="/wiki/SWAPO" title="SWAPO">SWAPO</a> in Namibia, and aiding opposition to the minority governments of <a href="/wiki/Mozambican_War_of_Independence" title="Mozambican War of Independence">Portuguese Mozambique</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Bush_War" title="Rhodesian Bush War">Rhodesia</a>. Nigeria retains membership in the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a>. In late November 2006, it organized an Africa-South America Summit in <a href="/wiki/Abuja" title="Abuja">Abuja</a> to promote what some attendees termed "South-South" linkages on a variety of fronts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> Nigeria is also a member of the <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>. It was temporarily expelled from the latter in 1995 when ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Sani_Abacha" title="Sani Abacha">Abacha regime</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:President_Barack_Obama_and_First_Lady_Michelle_Obama_greet_His_Excellency_Goodluck_Ebele_Jonathan,_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2640" data-file-width="3960" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/President_Barack_Obama_and_First_Lady_Michelle_Obama_greet_His_Excellency_Goodluck_Ebele_Jonathan%2C_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria.jpg/220px-President_Barack_Obama_and_First_Lady_Michelle_Obama_greet_His_Excellency_Goodluck_Ebele_Jonathan%2C_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/President_Barack_Obama_and_First_Lady_Michelle_Obama_greet_His_Excellency_Goodluck_Ebele_Jonathan%2C_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria.jpg/330px-President_Barack_Obama_and_First_Lady_Michelle_Obama_greet_His_Excellency_Goodluck_Ebele_Jonathan%2C_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/President_Barack_Obama_and_First_Lady_Michelle_Obama_greet_His_Excellency_Goodluck_Ebele_Jonathan%2C_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria.jpg/440px-President_Barack_Obama_and_First_Lady_Michelle_Obama_greet_His_Excellency_Goodluck_Ebele_Jonathan%2C_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:President_Barack_Obama_and_First_Lady_Michelle_Obama_greet_His_Excellency_Goodluck_Ebele_Jonathan,_President_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Nigeria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Former Nigerian President <a href="/wiki/Goodluck_Jonathan" title="Goodluck Jonathan">Goodluck Jonathan</a> (centre) with United States President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> and First Lady <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Obama" title="Michelle Obama">Michelle Obama</a> in August 2014</div></div></div>
<p>Nigeria has remained a key player in the <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry" title="Petroleum industry">international oil industry</a> since the 1970s and maintains membership in <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a>, which it joined in July 1971. Its <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry_in_Nigeria" title="Petroleum industry in Nigeria">status as a major petroleum producer</a> figures prominently in its sometimes volatile international relations with <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed countries</a>, notably the United States, and with developing countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since 2000, <a href="/wiki/China%E2%80%93Nigeria_relations" title="China–Nigeria relations">Chinese–Nigerian trade relations</a> have risen exponentially. There has been an increase in total trade of over 10,384 million dollars between the two nations from 2000 to 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> However, the structure of the Chinese–Nigerian trade relationship has become a major political issue for the Nigerian state. This is illustrated by the fact that Chinese exports account for around 80 per cent of total bilateral trade volumes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> This has resulted in a serious <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_trade" title="Balance of trade">trade imbalance</a>, with Nigeria importing ten times more than it exports to China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> Subsequently, Nigeria's economy is becoming over-reliant on cheap imports to sustain itself, resulting in a clear decline in Nigerian industry under such arrangements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>Continuing its Africa-centred foreign policy, Nigeria introduced the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Currency_union" title="Currency union">single currency</a> for West Africa known as the <a href="/wiki/Eco_(currency)" title="Eco (currency)">Eco</a> under the presumption that it would be led by the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_naira" title="Nigerian naira">naira</a>. But on 21 December 2019, <a href="/wiki/List_of_heads_of_state_of_Ivory_Coast" title="List of heads of state of Ivory Coast">Ivorian President</a> <a href="/wiki/Alassane_Ouattara" title="Alassane Ouattara">Alassane Ouattara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Macron" title="Emmanuel Macron">Emmanuel Macron</a>, and multiple other <a href="/wiki/Economic_Community_of_West_African_States" title="Economic Community of West African States">UEMOA</a> states announced that they would merely rename the <a href="/wiki/CFA_franc" title="CFA franc">CFA franc</a> instead of replacing the currency as originally intended. As of 2020, the Eco currency has been delayed to 2025.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Nigeria" title="Administrative divisions of Nigeria">Administrative divisions of Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Political_map_of_Nigeria.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="703" data-file-width="919" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Political_map_of_Nigeria.svg/220px-Political_map_of_Nigeria.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Political_map_of_Nigeria.svg/330px-Political_map_of_Nigeria.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Political_map_of_Nigeria.svg/440px-Political_map_of_Nigeria.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Political_map_of_Nigeria.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Nigeria with administrative divisions</div></div></div>
<p>Nigeria is divided into <a href="/wiki/States_of_Nigeria" title="States of Nigeria">thirty-six states</a> and one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Capital_Territory,_Nigeria" title="Federal Capital Territory, Nigeria">Federal Capital Territory</a>, which are further sub-divided into 774 <a href="/wiki/Local_government_areas_of_Nigeria" title="Local government areas of Nigeria">local government areas</a>. In some contexts, the states are aggregated into <a href="/wiki/Geopolitical_zones_of_Nigeria" title="Geopolitical zones of Nigeria">six geopolitical zones</a>: <a href="/wiki/North_West_(Nigeria)" title="North West (Nigeria)">North West</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_East_(Nigeria)" title="North East (Nigeria)">North East</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Central,_Nigeria" title="North Central, Nigeria">North Central</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_West_(Nigeria)" title="South West (Nigeria)">South West</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_East_(Nigeria)" title="South East (Nigeria)">South East</a>, and <a href="/wiki/South_South" title="South South">South South</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nigeria has five cities with a population of over a million (from largest to smallest): <a href="/wiki/Lagos" title="Lagos">Lagos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kano_(city)" title="Kano (city)">Kano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibadan" title="Ibadan">Ibadan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benin_City" title="Benin City">Benin City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Port_Harcourt" title="Port Harcourt">Port Harcourt</a>. Lagos is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_most_populous_cities_in_Africa" title="List of most populous cities in Africa">largest city in Africa</a>, with a population of over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_metropolitan_areas_by_population" title="List of metropolitan areas by population">12 million</a> in its <a href="/wiki/Urban_area" title="Urban area">urban area</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-felix2_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-felix2-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Nigeria" title="Geography of Nigeria">Geography of Nigeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Nigeria" title="Geology of Nigeria">Geology of Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nigeria_Topography.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1547" data-file-width="1708" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Nigeria_Topography.png/220px-Nigeria_Topography.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Nigeria_Topography.png/330px-Nigeria_Topography.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Nigeria_Topography.png/440px-Nigeria_Topography.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nigeria_Topography.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Topography of Nigeria</div></div></div>
<p>Nigeria is located in western Africa on the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Guinea" title="Gulf of Guinea">Gulf of Guinea</a> and has a total area of 923,768 km<sup>2</sup> (356,669 sq mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> making it the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area" title="List of countries and dependencies by area">32nd-largest country</a>. Its borders span 4,047 kilometres (2,515 mi), and it shares borders with Benin (773 km or 480 mi), Niger (1,497 km or 930 mi), Chad (87 km or 54 mi), and Cameroon (including the separatist <a href="/wiki/Ambazonia" title="Ambazonia">Ambazonia</a>) 1,690 km or 1,050 mi. Its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coastline" title="Coastline">coastline</a> is at least 853 km (530 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> Nigeria lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/4th_parallel_north" title="4th parallel north">4°</a> and <a href="/wiki/14th_parallel_north" title="14th parallel north">14°N</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/2nd_meridian_east" title="2nd meridian east">2°</a> and <a href="/wiki/15th_meridian_east" title="15th meridian east">15°E</a>. The highest point in Nigeria is <a href="/wiki/Chappal_Waddi" title="Chappal Waddi">Chappal Waddi</a> at 2,419 m (7,936 ft). The main rivers are the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Benue_River" title="Benue River">Benue</a>, which converge and empty into the <a href="/wiki/Niger_Delta" title="Niger Delta">Niger Delta</a>. This is one of the world's largest river deltas and the location of a large area of Central African <a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">mangroves</a>.
</p><p>Nigeria's most expansive topographical region is that of the valleys of the Niger and Benue river valleys (which merge and form a Y-shape).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encarta22_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-encarta22-190">[190]</a></sup> To the southwest of the Niger is a "rugged" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Highland_(geography)" title="Highland (geography)">highland</a>. To the southeast of the Benue are hills and mountains, which form the <a href="/wiki/Mambilla_Plateau" title="Mambilla Plateau">Mambilla Plateau</a>, the highest plateau in Nigeria. This plateau extends through the border with <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>, where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montane" title="Montane">montane</a> land is part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bamenda_Highlands" title="Bamenda Highlands">Bamenda Highlands</a> of Cameroon.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_NGA_present.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="1430" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Koppen-Geiger_Map_NGA_present.svg/220px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_NGA_present.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Koppen-Geiger_Map_NGA_present.svg/330px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_NGA_present.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Koppen-Geiger_Map_NGA_present.svg/440px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_NGA_present.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_NGA_present.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Climate map of Nigeria</div></div></div>
<p>Nigeria has a varied landscape. The far south is defined by its <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest_climate" title="Tropical rainforest climate">tropical rainforest climate</a>, where annual rainfall is 1,500 to 2,000 millimetres (60 to 80 in) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gai52_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-gai52-191">[191]</a></sup> In the southeast stands the <a href="/wiki/Obudu_Plateau" title="Obudu Plateau">Obudu Plateau</a>. <a href="/wiki/Coastal_plain" title="Coastal plain">Coastal plains</a> are found in both the southwest and the southeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encarta22_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-encarta22-190">[190]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mangrove_swamp" title="Mangrove swamp">Mangrove swamps</a> are found along the coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gai42_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-gai42-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p>The area near the border with Cameroon close to the coast is rich rainforest and part of the <a href="/wiki/Cross%E2%80%93Sanaga%E2%80%93Bioko_coastal_forests" title="Cross–Sanaga–Bioko coastal forests">Cross-Sanaga-Bioko coastal forests</a> <a href="/wiki/Ecoregion" title="Ecoregion">ecoregion</a>, an important centre for <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>. It is a habitat for the <a href="/wiki/Drill_(animal)" title="Drill (animal)">drill primate</a>, which is found in the wild only in this area and across the border in Cameroon. The areas surrounding <a href="/wiki/Calabar" title="Calabar">Calabar</a>, Cross River State, also in this forest, are believed to contain the world's largest diversity of butterflies. The area of southern Nigeria between the Niger and the <a href="/wiki/Cross_River_(Nigeria)" title="Cross River (Nigeria)">Cross Rivers</a> has lost most of its forest because of development and harvesting by increased population, and has been <a href="/wiki/Cross%E2%80%93Niger_transition_forests" title="Cross–Niger transition forests">replaced by grassland</a>.
</p><p>Everything in between the far south and the far north is <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savannah</a> (insignificant tree cover, with grasses and flowers located between trees). Rainfall is more limited to between 500 and 1,500 millimetres (20 and 60 in) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gai52_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-gai52-191">[191]</a></sup> The savannah zone's three categories are <a href="/wiki/Guinean_forest%E2%80%93savanna_mosaic" title="Guinean forest–savanna mosaic">Guinean forest-savanna mosaic</a>, Sudan savannah, and Sahel savannah. Guinean forest-savanna mosaic is plains of tall grass interrupted by trees. Sudan savannah is similar but with shorter grasses and shorter trees. Sahel savannah consists of patches of grass and sand, found in the northeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gai42_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-gai42-192">[192]</a></sup> In the Sahel region, rain is less than 500 millimetres (20 in) per year, and the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara Desert</a> is encroaching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gai52_191-2"><a href="#cite_note-gai52-191">[191]</a></sup> In the dry northeast corner of the country lies <a href="/wiki/Lake_Chad" title="Lake Chad">Lake Chad</a>, which Nigeria shares with Niger, Chad and Cameroon.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vegetation">Vegetation</span></h3>
