<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vegetation-nigeria-en.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1702" data-file-width="1667" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Vegetation-nigeria-en.jpg/220px-Vegetation-nigeria-en.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Vegetation-nigeria-en.jpg/330px-Vegetation-nigeria-en.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Vegetation-nigeria-en.jpg/440px-Vegetation-nigeria-en.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vegetation-nigeria-en.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Original vegetation (according to CIA map of 1979<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup>)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nigerian-deforrestation-b.jpg"><img alt="Nigerian deforrestation 1981 - 2020" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1370" data-file-width="968" decoding="async" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Nigerian-deforrestation-b.jpg/220px-Nigerian-deforrestation-b.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Nigerian-deforrestation-b.jpg/330px-Nigerian-deforrestation-b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Nigerian-deforrestation-b.jpg/440px-Nigerian-deforrestation-b.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nigerian-deforrestation-b.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Deforestation in Nigeria 1981 - 2020<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Nigeria is covered by three types of vegetation: forests (where there is significant tree cover), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Savannah" title="Savannah">savannahs</a> (insignificant tree cover, with grasses and flowers located between trees), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montane" title="Montane">montane</a> land (least common and mainly found in the mountains near the Cameroon border. Both the forest zone and the savannah zone are divided into three parts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gai4_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-gai4-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the forest zone's most southerly portion, especially around the Niger River and <a href="/wiki/Cross_River_(Nigeria)" title="Cross River (Nigeria)">Cross River</a> deltas, is <a href="/wiki/Central_African_mangroves" title="Central African mangroves">mangrove swamp</a>. North of this is fresh water swamp, containing different vegetation from the salt water mangrove swamps, and north of that is rain forest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gai4_195-1"><a href="#cite_note-gai4-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>The savannah zone's three categories are divided into <a href="/wiki/Guinean_forest%E2%80%93savanna_mosaic" title="Guinean forest–savanna mosaic">Guinean forest-savanna mosaic</a>, made up of plains of tall grass which are interrupted by trees, the most common across the country; Sudan savannah, with short grasses and short trees; and <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> savannah patches of grass and sand, found in the northeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gai4_195-2"><a href="#cite_note-gai4-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_issues">Environmental issues</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_spills">Oil spills</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issues_in_the_Niger_Delta" title="Environmental issues in the Niger Delta">Environmental issues in the Niger Delta</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rainforest_range_of_Obudu_Mountains.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Rainforest_range_of_Obudu_Mountains.jpg/220px-Rainforest_range_of_Obudu_Mountains.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Rainforest_range_of_Obudu_Mountains.jpg/330px-Rainforest_range_of_Obudu_Mountains.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Rainforest_range_of_Obudu_Mountains.jpg/440px-Rainforest_range_of_Obudu_Mountains.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rainforest_range_of_Obudu_Mountains.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rainforest range of <a href="/wiki/Obudu_Mountain_Resort" title="Obudu Mountain Resort">Obudu Mountains</a></div></div></div>
<p>Nigeria's Delta region, home of the large oil industry, experiences serious <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">oil spills</a> and other environmental problems, which has caused <a href="/wiki/Conflict_in_the_Niger_Delta" title="Conflict in the Niger Delta">conflict in the Delta region</a>.
</p><p>Illegal oil refineries, in which local operators convert stolen crude oil into petrol and diesel, are considered particularly "dirty, dangerous and lucrative"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup>. Safety and environmental aspects are usually ignored (e.g. no <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulphur</a> is removed from the fuels produced). Refining petroleum also inevitably produces heavy oil, which is "cracked" into lighter fuel components in regular plants at great technical expense. Illegal refineries do not have these technical possibilities and "dispose" of the heavy oil where it accumulates. The lighter components of crude oil (<a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> to <a href="/wiki/Butane" title="Butane">butane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isobutane" title="Isobutane">isobutane</a>), on the other hand, always mean a certain risk of explosion, which often leads to disasters at illegal plants<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup>. In 2022, Nigeria suffered 125 deaths from explosions at local, illegal refineries<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup>. An important aspect of the <a href="/wiki/Dangote_Refinery" title="Dangote Refinery">Dangote refinery</a> under construction is therefore that the oil will be transported from the <a href="/wiki/Oil_platform" title="Oil platform">offshore platforms</a> in the Niger Delta to Lagos via a 1,100 km long submarine pipeline along the coast. This is hoped to minimise oil theft and environmental damage.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deforestation">Deforestation</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Nigeria" title="Deforestation in Nigeria">Deforestation in Nigeria</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Waste_management" title="Waste management">Waste management</a> including <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">sewage treatment</a>, the linked processes of <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soil_retrogression_and_degradation" title="Soil retrogression and degradation">soil degradation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> are the major environmental problems in Nigeria. Waste management presents problems in a <a href="/wiki/Megacity" title="Megacity">megacity</a> like Lagos and other major Nigerian cities which are linked with economic development, population growth and the inability of municipal councils to manage the resulting rise in industrial and domestic waste. This waste management problem is also attributable to unsustainable environmental management lifestyles of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kubwa,_Abuja" title="Kubwa, Abuja">Kubwa</a> community in the Federal Capital Territory, where there are habits of indiscriminate disposal of waste, dumping of waste along or into the canals, sewerage systems that are channels for water flows, and the like. Haphazard industrial planning, increased urbanisation, poverty and lack of competence of the municipal government are seen as the major reasons for high levels of waste pollution in major cities of the country. Some of the solutions have been disastrous to the environment, resulting in untreated waste being dumped in places where it can pollute waterways and <a href="/wiki/Groundwater_in_Nigeria" title="Groundwater in Nigeria">groundwater</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2005, Nigeria had the highest rate of <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> in the world, according to the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> That year, 12.2%, the equivalent of 11,089,000 hectares had been forested in the country. Between 1990 and 2000, Nigeria lost an average of 409,700 hectares of forest every year equal to an average annual deforestation rate of 2.4%. Between 1990 and 2005, in total Nigeria lost 35.7% of its <a href="/wiki/Forest_cover" title="Forest cover">forest cover</a> or around 6,145,000 hectares.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> Nigeria had a 2019 <a href="/wiki/Forest_Landscape_Integrity_Index" title="Forest Landscape Integrity Index">Forest Landscape Integrity Index</a> mean score of 6.2/10, ranking it 82nd globally out of 172 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FLII-Supplementary_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FLII-Supplementary-202">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the year 2010, thousands of people were inadvertently <a href="/wiki/Zamfara_State_lead_poisoning_outbreak" title="Zamfara State lead poisoning outbreak">exposed to lead-containing soil</a> from informal <a href="/wiki/Gold_mining" title="Gold mining">gold mining</a> within the northern state of Zamfara. While estimates vary, it is thought that upwards of 400 children died of acute <a href="/wiki/Lead_poisoning" title="Lead poisoning">lead poisoning</a>, making this perhaps the largest lead poisoning fatality outbreak ever encountered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Nigeria" title="Economy of Nigeria">Economy of Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nigeria_Product_Exports_(2019).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="715" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Nigeria_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg/220px-Nigeria_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Nigeria_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg/330px-Nigeria_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Nigeria_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg/440px-Nigeria_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nigeria_Product_Exports_(2019).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A proportional representation of Nigeria exports, 2019</div></div></div>
<p>Nigeria's <a href="/wiki/Mixed_economy" title="Mixed economy">mixed economy</a> is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_African_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of African countries by GDP (nominal)">largest in Africa</a>, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal)">31st-largest in the world</a> by nominal GDP, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP)">30th-largest</a> by <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">PPP</a>. It is a lower-middle-income economy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> with its abundant supply of natural resources, well-developed financial, legal, communications, transport sectors and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nigerian_Stock_Exchange" title="Nigerian Stock Exchange">Nigerian Stock Exchange</a>. Economic development has been hindered by years of <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship" title="Military dictatorship">military rule</a>, corruption, and mismanagement. The restoration of democracy and subsequent economic reforms have successfully put Nigeria back on track towards achieving its full economic potential. Next to petroleum, the second-largest source of foreign exchange earnings for Nigeria are <a href="/wiki/Remittance" title="Remittance">remittances</a> sent home by Nigerians living abroad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tribune.com.ng2_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-tribune.com.ng2-205">[205]</a></sup> In addition to its petroleum resources, Nigeria also has a wide array of underexploited mineral resources which include coal, <a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">bauxite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tantalite" title="Tantalite">tantalite</a>, gold, <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, iron ore, <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niobium" title="Niobium">niobium</a>, lead and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gov2_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gov2-206">[206]</a></sup> Despite huge deposits of these natural resources, the mining industry in Nigeria is still in its infancy.
</p><p>During the oil boom of the 1970s, Nigeria accumulated a significant foreign debt to finance major infrastructural investments. With the fall of oil prices during the <a href="/wiki/1980s_oil_glut" title="1980s oil glut">1980s oil glut</a>, Nigeria struggled to keep up with its loan payments and eventually defaulted on its principal debt repayments, limiting repayment to the interest portion of the loans. Arrears and penalty interest accumulated on the unpaid principal, which increased the size of the debt. After negotiations by the Nigerian authorities, in October 2005 Nigeria and its <a href="/wiki/Paris_Club" title="Paris Club">Paris Club</a> creditors reached an agreement under which Nigeria repurchased its debt at a discount of approximately 60%. Nigeria used part of its oil profits to pay the residual 40%, freeing up at least $1.15 billion annually for poverty reduction programmes. Nigeria made history in April 2006 by becoming the first African country to completely pay off its debt (estimated $30 billion) owed to the Paris Club.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Nigeria" title="Agriculture in Nigeria">Agriculture in Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palm_nuts_put_out_to_dry.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3552" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Palm_nuts_put_out_to_dry.jpg/220px-Palm_nuts_put_out_to_dry.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Palm_nuts_put_out_to_dry.jpg/330px-Palm_nuts_put_out_to_dry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Palm_nuts_put_out_to_dry.jpg/440px-Palm_nuts_put_out_to_dry.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Palm_nuts_put_out_to_dry.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Palm nuts put out to dry</div></div></div>
<p>As of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nigeria&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, about 30% of Nigerians are employed in agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> About 26.5% of Nigeria's GDP is contributed by its agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> As far as <a href="/wiki/Cassava_production_in_Nigeria" title="Cassava production in Nigeria">cassava</a> is concerned, Nigeria is the world's largest producer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-210">[210]</a></sup> Further major crops include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beans" title="Beans">beans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sesame" title="Sesame">sesame</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cashew_nuts" title="Cashew nuts">cashew nuts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cocoa_beans" title="Cocoa beans">cocoa beans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bambara_groundnut" title="Bambara groundnut">groundnuts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gum_arabic" title="Gum arabic">gum arabic</a>, kolanut, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> (corn), <a href="/wiki/Melon" title="Melon">melon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palm_kernel" title="Palm kernel">palm kernels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plantain_(cooking)" title="Plantain (cooking)">plantains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rubber" title="Rubber">rubber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soybeans" title="Soybeans">soybeans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yam_(vegetable)" title="Yam (vegetable)">yams</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nigeria_agriculture2_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nigeria_agriculture2-211">[211]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">Cocoa</a> is the leading non-oil foreign exchange earner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nigeria_agriculture2_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nigeria_agriculture2-211">[211]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rubber" title="Rubber">Rubber</a> is the second-largest non-oil foreign exchange earner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nigeria_agriculture2_211-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nigeria_agriculture2-211">[211]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Imota-rice-mill-20220817c.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="946" data-file-width="1679" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Imota-rice-mill-20220817c.jpg/220px-Imota-rice-mill-20220817c.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Imota-rice-mill-20220817c.jpg/330px-Imota-rice-mill-20220817c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Imota-rice-mill-20220817c.jpg/440px-Imota-rice-mill-20220817c.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Imota-rice-mill-20220817c.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Imota_rice_mill" title="Imota rice mill">Imota Rice Mill</a>, close to Lagos</div></div></div>
<p>Before the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Civil_War" title="Nigerian Civil War">Nigerian civil war</a>, Nigeria was self-sufficient in food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nigeria_agriculture2_211-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nigeria_agriculture2-211">[211]</a></sup> Agriculture used to be the principal foreign exchange earner of Nigeria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> Agriculture has failed to keep pace with Nigeria's rapid population growth, and Nigeria now relies upon food imports to sustain itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nigeria_agriculture2_211-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nigeria_agriculture2-211">[211]</a></sup> It spends US$6.7 billion yearly for food imports, four times more than revenues from food export.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-210">[210]</a></sup> The Nigerian government promoted the use of inorganic fertilizers in the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pasquini2_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-pasquini2-213">[213]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nigeria's rice production increased by 10% from 2017/18 to 2021/22 to 5 million tonnes a year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> but could hardly keep up with the increased demand. Rice imports therefore remained constant at 2 million tonnes per year.  In August 2019, Nigeria closed its border with Benin and other neighbouring countries to stop rice smuggling into the country as part of efforts to boost local production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p>Until now, Nigeria exported unhusked rice but had to import husked rice, the country's staple food. - The <a href="/wiki/Imota_rice_mill" title="Imota rice mill">rice mill in Imota</a>, near Lagos, is intended to handle the corresponding processing at home, improve the balance of trade and the labour market, and save unnecessary costs for transport and middlemen. When fully operational at the end of 2022, the plant, the largest south of the Sahara, is expected to employ 250,000 people and produce 2.5 million 50-kg bags of rice annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_fuels">Fossil fuels</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry_in_Nigeria" title="Petroleum industry in Nigeria">Petroleum industry in Nigeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_oil_exports" title="List of countries by oil exports">List of countries by oil exports</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nigeria-oil-n-gas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1592" data-file-width="1877" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Nigeria-oil-n-gas.jpg/220px-Nigeria-oil-n-gas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Nigeria-oil-n-gas.jpg/330px-Nigeria-oil-n-gas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Nigeria-oil-n-gas.jpg/440px-Nigeria-oil-n-gas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nigeria-oil-n-gas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oil and gas fields in the Niger delta</div></div></div>
<p>Nigeria is the 12th largest producer of petroleum in the world, the 8th largest exporter, and has the 10th largest proven reserves. Petroleum plays a large role in the Nigerian economy, accounting for 40% of GDP and 80% of government earnings. However, agitation for better resource control in the <a href="/wiki/Niger_Delta" title="Niger Delta">Niger Delta</a>, its main oil-producing region, has led to disruptions in oil production and prevents the country from exporting at 100% capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup>
The Niger Delta Nembe Creek oil field was discovered in 1973 and produces from middle <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">deltaic</a> <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a>-<a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shale</a> in an <a href="/wiki/Anticline" title="Anticline">anticline</a> <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_trap" title="Petroleum trap">structural trap</a> at a depth of 2 to 4 kilometres (7,000 to 13,000 feet).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> In June 2013, Shell announced a strategic review of its operations in Nigeria, hinting that assets could be divested. While many international oil companies have operated there for decades, by 2014 most were making moves to divest their interests, citing a range of issues including oil theft. In August 2014, Shell said it was finalising its interests in four Nigerian oil fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShellNigeria2_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShellNigeria2-219">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nigeria has a total of 159 <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_reservoir" title="Petroleum reservoir">oil fields</a> and 1,481 <a href="/wiki/Oil_well" title="Oil well">wells</a> in operation according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Department_of_Petroleum_Resources" title="Department of Petroleum Resources">Department of Petroleum Resources</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NDES2_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-NDES2-220">[220]</a></sup> The most productive region of the nation is the coastal <a href="/wiki/Niger_Delta_Basin_(geology)" title="Niger Delta Basin (geology)">Niger Delta Basin</a> in the Niger Delta or "south-south" region which encompasses 78 of the 159 oil fields. Most of Nigeria's oil fields are small and scattered, and as of 1990, these small fields accounted for 62.1% of all Nigerian production. This contrasts with the sixteen largest fields which produced 37.9% of Nigeria's petroleum at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-politicaleconomy2_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-politicaleconomy2-221">[221]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Column-Dangote-refinery-nig2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1887" data-file-width="2399" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Column-Dangote-refinery-nig2.jpg/220px-Column-Dangote-refinery-nig2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Column-Dangote-refinery-nig2.jpg/330px-Column-Dangote-refinery-nig2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Column-Dangote-refinery-nig2.jpg/440px-Column-Dangote-refinery-nig2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Column-Dangote-refinery-nig2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The world's biggest distilling column at the <a href="/wiki/Dangote_Refinery" title="Dangote Refinery">Dangote refinery</a> in comparison</div></div></div>
<p>Petrol was Nigeria's main import commodity until 2021, accounting for 24% of import volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> - In the fourth quarter of 2022, the much talked about Dangote refinery will come on stream, producing 50 million litres of petrol per day, among other things.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> This would turn Nigeria from a net importer to a net exporter of petroleum products. The Dangote refinery can lay claim to a number of superlatives even before it is operational, including the world's largest distillation column<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> and, with the RFCC regenerator, both the largest continuous piece of steel (made by a Korean forge) and the heaviest object ever transported on an African road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup>
</p><p>The supply of natural gas to Europe, threatened by the Ukraine war, is pushing projects to transport Nigerian natural gas via pipelines to Morocco or Algeria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> As of May 2022, however, there are no results on this yet.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manufacturing_and_technology">Manufacturing and technology</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry_in_Nigeria" title="Automotive industry in Nigeria">Automotive industry in Nigeria</a></div>
<p>Nigeria has a manufacturing industry that includes leather and textiles (centred in <a href="/wiki/Kano_(city)" title="Kano (city)">Kano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abeokuta" title="Abeokuta">Abeokuta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Onitsha" title="Onitsha">Onitsha</a>, and Lagos), plastics and processed food. <a href="/wiki/Ogun_State" title="Ogun State">Ogun</a> is considered to be Nigeria's current industrial hub, as most factories are located in Ogun and more companies are moving there, followed by Lagos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup>
</p><p>The city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aba,_Abia" title="Aba, Abia">Aba</a> in the south-eastern part of the country is well known for handicrafts and shoes, known as "Aba made".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naijalitz_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naijalitz-235">[235]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016 (the last year from which data is available), Nigeria was the <a href="/wiki/Cement_production_by_country" title="Cement production by country">leading cement producer</a> south of the Sahara, ahead of South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aliko_Dangote" title="Aliko Dangote">Aliko Dangote</a>, Nigeria's richest inhabitant, based his wealth on cement production.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ara-mk.-iii-mrap.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="1159" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ara-mk.-iii-mrap.jpg/220px-Ara-mk.-iii-mrap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ara-mk.-iii-mrap.jpg/330px-Ara-mk.-iii-mrap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ara-mk.-iii-mrap.jpg/440px-Ara-mk.-iii-mrap.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ara-mk.-iii-mrap.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ara Mk. III MRAP, the first vehicle exported in larger numbers to Europe, designed and built in Nigeria</div></div></div>
<p>Nigeria has a market of 720,000 cars per year, but less than 20% of these are produced domestically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-237">[237]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 3 May 2022 a <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertiliser</a> production plant was commissioned near Lagos that will produce 3 million tonnes of fertiliser a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-african.business_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-african.business-239">[239]</a></sup> With no more Russian fertiliser coming onto the world market in 2022 due to the <a href="/wiki/2022_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine" title="2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine">Ukraine war</a>, Nigeria is filling a gap in the market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-african.business_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-african.business-239">[239]</a></sup>
</p><p>Currently, Nigeria hosts about 60 percent of the pharmaceutical production capacity in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> Most larger pharmaceutical companies in Nigeria are located in <a href="/wiki/Lagos" title="Lagos">Lagos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> The pharmaceutical producer with the most employees in Nigeria appears to be <i>Emzor Pharmaceutical Industries Ltd</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> Furthermore, <i>Fidson Healthcare Plc</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> <i>May &amp; Baker Nig. Plc</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup> and <i>Swiss Pharma Nigeria</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> seem to be the major pharmaceutical companies in Nigeria.
</p><p>Nigeria has a few electronic manufacturers like Zinox, the first branded Nigerian computer, and manufacturers of electronic gadgets such as tablet PCs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ajaokuta-factory.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="702" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Ajaokuta-factory.JPG/220px-Ajaokuta-factory.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Ajaokuta-factory.JPG/330px-Ajaokuta-factory.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Ajaokuta-factory.JPG/440px-Ajaokuta-factory.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ajaokuta-factory.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Steel factory in Ajaokuta</div></div></div>
<p>According to its own information, the <a href="/wiki/Ajaokuta_Steel_Mill" title="Ajaokuta Steel Mill">Ajaokuta Steel Company Limited</a> produces 1.3 million tonnes of <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup> This would be equivalent to one-sixth of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">UK</a>'s steel production in 2021.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> for the steel production originates from the ore mine in <a href="/wiki/Itakpe" title="Itakpe">Itakpe</a>.
</p><p>Steel plants in <a href="/wiki/Katsina" title="Katsina">Katsina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jos" title="Jos">Jos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osogbo" title="Osogbo">Osogbo</a> no longer appear to be active.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Services">Services</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Banking_in_Nigeria" title="Banking in Nigeria">Banking in Nigeria</a></div>
<p>Nigeria has a highly developed financial services sector, with a mix of local and international banks, asset management companies, brokerage houses, insurance companies and brokers, private equity funds and investment banks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Nigeria" title="Telecommunications in Nigeria">Telecommunications in Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Play_and_Learn.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="713" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Play_and_Learn.png/220px-Play_and_Learn.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Play_and_Learn.png/330px-Play_and_Learn.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Play_and_Learn.png/440px-Play_and_Learn.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Play_and_Learn.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Telecommunication: the Nigerian Way-of-Life</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Nigeria" title="Telecommunications in Nigeria">Telecommunications in Nigeria</a>  is a part of the Nigerian way-of-life. According to a survey by the GSM Association, 92% of adult Nigerian men own a mobile phone (for women this is 88%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of January 2022, there were 109.2 million internet users in Nigeria. Nigeria's internet penetration rate was thus 51.0% of the total population at the beginning of 2022. Furthermore, the number of internet users in Nigeria increased by 4.8 million (+4.6 per cent) between 2021 and 2022. Internet users in Nigeria had 17.38 Mbps median mobile internet connection speed at the beginning of 2022. The median mobile internet connection speed in Nigeria increased by 4.88 Mbps (+39.0 per cent) in the 12 months to early 2022.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-252">[252]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of January 2022, there were 32.90 million social media users in Nigeria. This represented 15.4 per cent of the total population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_252-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-252">[252]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nigeria has one of the fastest-growing telecommunications markets in the world, with major emerging market operators (like <a href="/wiki/MTN_Group" title="MTN Group">MTN</a>, 9mobile, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airtel" title="Airtel">Airtel</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Globacom" title="Globacom">Globacom</a>) basing their largest and most profitable centres in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> Nigeria's ICT sector has experienced a lot of growth, representing 10% of the nation's GDP in 2018 as compared to just 1% in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup> Lagos is regarded as one of the largest technology hubs in Africa with its thriving tech ecosystem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup> Several startups like Paystack, Interswitch, Bolt and Piggyvest are leveraging technology to solve issues across different sectors.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Nigeria" title="Transport in Nigeria">Transport in Nigeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_Niger_bridge" title="Second Niger bridge">Second Niger bridge</a></div>
<p>Due to Nigeria's location in the centre of Africa, transport plays a major role in the national service sector. - The government under <a href="/wiki/Muhammadu_Buhari" title="Muhammadu Buhari">Buhari</a> initiated improvements to the infrastructure after 2015. Extensive road repairs and new construction have been carried out gradually as states in particular spend their share of increased government allocations. Representative of these improvements is the <a href="/wiki/Second_Niger_bridge" title="Second Niger bridge">Second Niger Bridge</a> near Onitsha, which was largely completed in 2022.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Nigeria" title="Tourism in Nigeria">Tourism in Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meridien_Akwa_Ibom_park.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Meridien_Akwa_Ibom_park.jpg/220px-Meridien_Akwa_Ibom_park.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Meridien_Akwa_Ibom_park.jpg/330px-Meridien_Akwa_Ibom_park.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Meridien_Akwa_Ibom_park.jpg/440px-Meridien_Akwa_Ibom_park.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meridien_Akwa_Ibom_park.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Meridien <a href="/wiki/Akwa_Ibom_State" title="Akwa Ibom State">Akwa Ibom</a> golf course park</div></div></div>
<p>Tourism in Nigeria centres largely on events, because of the country's ample amount of ethnic groups, but also includes rain forests, savannah, waterfalls, and other natural attractions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suntra2_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-suntra2-257">[257]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lekki_Beach_in_Lagos.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1454" data-file-width="2456" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Lekki_Beach_in_Lagos.jpg/220px-Lekki_Beach_in_Lagos.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Lekki_Beach_in_Lagos.jpg/330px-Lekki_Beach_in_Lagos.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Lekki_Beach_in_Lagos.jpg/440px-Lekki_Beach_in_Lagos.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lekki_Beach_in_Lagos.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lekki Beach in Lagos</div></div></div>
<p>Abuja is home to several parks and green areas. The largest, <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Park_(Abuja)" title="Millennium Park (Abuja)">Millennium Park</a>, was designed by architect <a href="/wiki/Manfredi_Nicoletti" title="Manfredi Nicoletti">Manfredi Nicoletti</a> and officially opened in December 2003. After the re-modernization project achieved by the administration of Governor Raji Babatunde Fashola, Lagos is gradually becoming a major tourist destination. Lagos is currently taking steps to become a <a href="/wiki/Global_city" title="Global city">global city</a>. The 2009 Eyo carnival (a yearly festival originating from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iperu,_Ogun" title="Iperu, Ogun">Iperu Remo</a>, Ogun State) was a step toward world city status. Currently, Lagos is primarily known as a business-oriented and fast-paced community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-METRO2_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-METRO2-258">[258]</a></sup> Lagos has become an important location for African and black cultural identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many festivals are held in Lagos; festivals vary in offerings each year and may be held in different months. Some of the festivals are <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20180820075714/http://www.festacfoodfair.com/" rel="nofollow">Festac Food Fair held in Festac</a> Town Annually, Eyo Festival, <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20181102044321/http://lagosblackheritagefestival.com/" rel="nofollow">Lagos Black Heritage Carnival</a>, Lagos Carnival, Eko International Film Festival, Lagos Seafood Festac Festival, LAGOS PHOTO Festival and the Lagos Jazz Series, which is a unique franchise for high-quality live music in all genres with a focus on jazz. Established in 2010, the event takes place over a 3 to 5 days period at selected high-quality outdoor venues. The music is as varied as the audience itself and features a diverse mix of musical genres from rhythm and blues to soul, <a href="/wiki/Afrobeat" title="Afrobeat">Afrobeat</a>, hip hop, bebop, and traditional jazz. The festivals provide entertainment of dance and song to add excitement to travellers during a stay in Lagos.
</p><p>Lagos has sandy beaches by the Atlantic Ocean, including <a href="/wiki/Elegushi_Beach" title="Elegushi Beach">Elegushi Beach</a> and Alpha Beach. Lagos also has many private beach resorts including Inagbe Grand Beach Resort and several others in the outskirts. Lagos has a variety of hotels ranging from three-star to five-star hotels, with a mixture of local hotels such as <a href="/wiki/Eko_Hotels_and_Suites" title="Eko Hotels and Suites">Eko Hotels and Suites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Federal_Palace_Hotel" title="Federal Palace Hotel">Federal Palace Hotel</a> and franchises of multinational chains such as Intercontinental Hotel, Sheraton, and Four Points by Hilton. Other places of interest include the <a href="/wiki/Tafawa_Balewa_Square" title="Tafawa Balewa Square">Tafawa Balewa Square</a>, Festac town, The <a href="/wiki/Nike_Art_Gallery" title="Nike Art Gallery">Nike Art Gallery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freedom_Park_(Lagos)" title="Freedom Park (Lagos)">Freedom Park</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_Church_of_Christ,_Lagos" title="Cathedral Church of Christ, Lagos">Cathedral Church of Christ</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">Infrastructure</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Nigeria" title="Energy in Nigeria">Energy in Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abuja_road.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1107" data-file-width="3109" decoding="async" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Abuja_road.jpg/220px-Abuja_road.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Abuja_road.jpg/330px-Abuja_road.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Abuja_road.jpg/440px-Abuja_road.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abuja_road.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Substation in Abuja</div></div></div><p>Nigeria's primary energy consumption was about 108 <a href="/wiki/Tonne_of_oil_equivalent" title="Tonne of oil equivalent">Mtoe</a> in 2011. Most of the energy comes from traditional <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> and waste, which account for 83% of total primary production. The rest is from fossil fuels (16%) and hydropower (1%). Since independence, Nigeria has tried to develop a domestic nuclear industry for energy. Since 2004, Nigeria has had a Chinese-origin research reactor at <a href="/wiki/Ahmadu_Bello_University" title="Ahmadu Bello University">Ahmadu Bello University</a> and has sought the support of the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">International Atomic Energy Agency</a> to develop plans for up to 4,000 MWe of nuclear capacity by 2027 according to the National Program for the Deployment of Nuclear Power for Generation of Electricity. In 2007, President <a href="/wiki/Umaru_Musa_Yar%27Adua" title="Umaru Musa Yar'Adua">Umaru Yar'Adua</a> urged the country to embrace nuclear power to meet its growing energy needs. In 2017, Nigeria signed the UN <a href="/wiki/Treaty_on_the_Prohibition_of_Nuclear_Weapons" title="Treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons">treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2015, Nigeria began talks with Russia's state-owned <a href="/wiki/Rosatom" title="Rosatom">Rosatom</a> to collaborate on the design, construction and operation of four nuclear power plants by 2035, the first of which will be in operation by 2025. In June 2015, Nigeria selected two sites for the planned construction of the nuclear plants. Neither the Nigerian government nor Rosatom would disclose the specific locations of the sites, but it is believed that the nuclear plants will be sited in <a href="/wiki/Akwa_Ibom_State" title="Akwa Ibom State">Akwa Ibom State</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kogi_State" title="Kogi State">Kogi State</a>. The sites are planned to house two plants each. In 2017 agreements were signed for the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Itu_nuclear_power_plant" title="Itu nuclear power plant">Itu nuclear power plant</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation_2">Transportation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transportation_in_Nigeria" title="Transportation in Nigeria">Transportation in Nigeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_Niger_bridge" title="Second Niger bridge">Second Niger bridge</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nigeria_Highways_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="521" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Nigeria_Highways_01.jpg/220px-Nigeria_Highways_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Nigeria_Highways_01.jpg/330px-Nigeria_Highways_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Nigeria_Highways_01.jpg/440px-Nigeria_Highways_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nigeria_Highways_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Highway A1 at Lagos-Onipanu with bus lane and in the background a pedestrian bridge</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2ndbridge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="445" data-file-width="791" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/2ndbridge.jpg/220px-2ndbridge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/2ndbridge.jpg/330px-2ndbridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/2ndbridge.jpg/440px-2ndbridge.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2ndbridge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Second Niger bridge at Onitsha, artistic impression</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Roads">Roads</span></h4>
<p>Nigeria has the largest road network in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>. It covers about 200,000 km, of which 60,000 km are asphalted. Nigeria's roads and highways handle 90% of all passenger and freight traffic. It contributes N2.4trn ($6.4bn) to GDP in 2020. 
</p><p>35,000 km of the road network fall under the jurisdiction of the federal government. Under the second <a href="/wiki/Muhammadu_Buhari" title="Muhammadu Buhari">Buhari</a> administration, the budget for maintenance and paving of these 35,000 km was almost doubled in regular increments from 295 billion naira ($819 million) in 2018 to 563 billion naira ($1.3 billion) in 2022. Responsible for this budget (as of September 2022) is Minister <a href="/wiki/Babatunde_Fashola" title="Babatunde Fashola">Fashola</a>, one of Nigeria's most respected politicians. Under him, the motorway links of important economic centres such as <a href="/wiki/Lagos" title="Lagos">Lagos</a>-<a href="/wiki/Ibadan" title="Ibadan">Ibadan</a>, Lagos-<a href="/wiki/Badagry" title="Badagry">Badagry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enugu" title="Enugu">Enugu</a>-<a href="/wiki/Onitsha" title="Onitsha">Onitsha</a> have been renovated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup>
</p><p>The rest of the road network is a state matter and therefore in very different shape, depending on which state you are in. Surprisingly, economically strong states such as Lagos, <a href="/wiki/Anambra_State" title="Anambra State">Anambra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rivers_State" title="Rivers State">Rivers</a> receive particularly poor evaluations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup> Most roads were built in the 1980s and early 1990s. Poor maintenance and inferior materials have worsened the condition of the roads. Travelling is very difficult. Especially during the rainy season, the use of secondary roads is sometimes almost impossible due to potholes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup> Road bandits often take advantage of this situation for their criminal purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Railways">Railways</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Railway_Corporation" title="Nigerian Railway Corporation">Nigerian Railway Corporation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Railway_system_Nigeria_2022_english.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1990" data-file-width="2347" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Railway_system_Nigeria_2022_english.jpg/220px-Railway_system_Nigeria_2022_english.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Railway_system_Nigeria_2022_english.jpg/330px-Railway_system_Nigeria_2022_english.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Railway_system_Nigeria_2022_english.jpg/440px-Railway_system_Nigeria_2022_english.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Railway_system_Nigeria_2022_english.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Railway system in Nigeria, 2022</div></div></div>
<p>The railways have undergone a massive revamping with projects such as the <a href="/wiki/Lagos%E2%80%93Kano_Standard_Gauge_Railway" title="Lagos–Kano Standard Gauge Railway">Lagos-Kano Standard Gauge Railway</a> being completed connecting northern cities of Kano, Kaduna, Abuja, Ibadan and Lagos.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_traffic">Air traffic</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_airports_in_Nigeria" title="List of airports in Nigeria">List of airports in Nigeria</a></div>
<p>There are 54 airports in Nigeria; the principal airports are
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Murtala_Muhammed_International_Airport" title="Murtala Muhammed International Airport">Murtala Muhammed International Airport</a> in Lagos,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nnamdi_Azikiwe_International_Airport" title="Nnamdi Azikiwe International Airport">Nnamdi Azikiwe International Airport</a> in Abuja,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mallam_Aminu_Kano_International_Airport" title="Mallam Aminu Kano International Airport">Mallam Aminu Kano International Airport</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kano_(city)" title="Kano (city)">Kano</a>,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Akanu_Ibiam_International_Airport" title="Akanu Ibiam International Airport">Akanu Ibiam International Airport</a> in <a href="/wiki/Enugu" title="Enugu">Enugu</a> and</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Port_Harcourt_International_Airport" title="Port Harcourt International Airport">Port Harcourt International Airport</a> in <a href="/wiki/Port_Harcourt" title="Port Harcourt">Port Harcourt</a>.</li></ul>
<p>In December 2021, the <a href="/wiki/Anambra_International_Cargo_Airport" title="Anambra International Cargo Airport">Anambra International Cargo Airport</a> started its operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2022, the second terminal of the <a href="/wiki/Murtala_Muhammed_International_Airport" title="Murtala Muhammed International Airport">Murtalla Muhammed International Airport</a> has been inaugurated. It will increase the capacity of the airport to 14 million passengers per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2022, the <a href="/wiki/Akwa_Ibom_Airport" title="Akwa Ibom Airport">Akwa Ibom International Airport</a> will open the most modern terminal in Nigeria so far.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>"Air Nigeria", a new effort</i>
</p><p>On 24 November 2021, the Federal Minister of Aviation Sirika announced that his government wanted to re-launch Air Nigeria, the national airline that was dissolved in 2012. To this end, he said, the Buhari administration would provide "150 to 300 million USD". Ultimately, the state's share in the company's capital is to be 5%. 46% would come from local investors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2022, it was announced that Nigeria will launch the national carrier Nigeria Air with three wet-lease aircraft. Wet lease means renting an aircraft, including crew and possibly fuel. Both Airbus and Boeing aircraft will be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup> The federal government will spend an average of €100,000 per month to lease an aircraft, which amounts to €300,000 per month for the three aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_traffic">Sea traffic</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lekki_Port" title="Lekki Port">Lekki Port</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Harbourseaside-lekki-port4en.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1089" data-file-width="1923" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Harbourseaside-lekki-port4en.jpg/220px-Harbourseaside-lekki-port4en.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Harbourseaside-lekki-port4en.jpg/330px-Harbourseaside-lekki-port4en.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Harbourseaside-lekki-port4en.jpg/440px-Harbourseaside-lekki-port4en.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Harbourseaside-lekki-port4en.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nigerias first sea port with 16,5m depth in Lekki, July 2022</div></div></div>
<p>So far, there is no port in Nigeria with a depth of more than 13 metres. This is set to change with the Lekki Deep Sea Port. The port is scheduled to be fully completed in September 2022 and to become operational at the end of 2022.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup> It has a depth of 16.5m, can serve Post-Panamax-vessels and will increase Nigeria's port capacity fivefold.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Telecommunications">Telecommunications</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Nigeria" title="Telecommunications in Nigeria">Telecommunications in Nigeria</a></div>
<p>According to the National Bureau of Statistics in 2020, Nigeria has about 136,203,231 internet users out of an estimated population of 205,886,311.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup> This implies that as of 2020, 66 per cent of the Nigerian population are connected to the internet and using it actively. Although Nigerians are using the internet for educational, social networking, and entertainment purposes, the internet has also become a tool for mobilizing political protests in Nigeria. However, the Nigerian government has become threatened by how its citizens are using the internet to influence governance and political changes. Using various measures including but not limited to Illegal arrest, taking down of websites, passport seizures, and restricted access to bank accounts, the Nigerian Government punishes citizens for expressing themselves on the internet and working to stifle internet freedom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to how the Nigerian government is responding to internet freedom among other things such as limitations to internet access and violations of users rights, Nigeria ranked 26th out of the 65 countries evaluated for internet freedom in the Freedom House 2020 Index.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_satellites">Government satellites</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Launch_of_CRS-11_mission.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Launch_of_CRS-11_mission.jpg/220px-Launch_of_CRS-11_mission.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Launch_of_CRS-11_mission.jpg/330px-Launch_of_CRS-11_mission.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Launch_of_CRS-11_mission.jpg/440px-Launch_of_CRS-11_mission.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Launch_of_CRS-11_mission.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a> launch of CRS-11 with <a href="/wiki/Nigeria_EduSat-1" title="Nigeria EduSat-1">Nigeria EduSat-1</a> on board in 2017</div></div></div>
<p>The government has recently begun expanding this infrastructure to <a href="/wiki/National_Space_Research_and_Development_Agency" title="National Space Research and Development Agency">space-based communications</a>. Nigeria has a space satellite that is monitored at the Nigerian National Space Research and Development Agency Headquarters in Abuja. The Nigerian government has commissioned the overseas production and launch of four satellites.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/NigComSat-1" title="NigComSat-1">NigComSat-1</a> was the first Nigerian satellite built, was Nigeria's third satellite, and Africa's first communication satellite. It was launched in 2007 aboard a Chinese <a href="/wiki/Long_March_3B" title="Long March 3B">Long March 3B</a> <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">carrier rocket</a>, from the <a href="/wiki/Xichang_Satellite_Launch_Center" title="Xichang Satellite Launch Center">Xichang Satellite Launch Center</a> in China. The spacecraft was operated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NIGCOMSAT" title="NIGCOMSAT">NigComSat</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Space_Research_and_Development_Agency" title="National Space Research and Development Agency">Nigerian Space Research and Development Agency</a>. On 11 November 2008, NigComSat-1 failed in orbit after running out of power because of an anomaly in its solar array. It was based on the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Dongfanghong_program" title="Dongfanghong program">DFH-4</a> <a href="/wiki/Satellite_bus" title="Satellite bus">satellite bus</a> and carries a variety of <a href="/wiki/Transponder" title="Transponder">transponders</a>: four <a href="/wiki/C_band_(IEEE)" title="C band (IEEE)">C-band</a>; fourteen <a href="/wiki/Ku_band" title="Ku band">Ku-band</a>; eight <a href="/wiki/Ka_band" title="Ka band">Ka-band</a>; and two <a href="/wiki/L_band" title="L band">L-band</a>. It was designed to provide coverage to many parts of Africa, and the Ka-band transponders would also cover Italy. The satellite was launched from Russia on 27 September 2003. Nigeriasat-1 was part of the worldwide Disaster Monitoring Constellation System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup> The primary objectives of the Nigeriasat-1 were: to give early warning signals of environmental disaster; to help detect and control <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a> in the northern part of Nigeria; to assist in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demographic" title="Demographic">demographic</a> planning; to establish the relationship between <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> vectors and the environment that breeds malaria and to give early warning signals on future outbreaks of <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">meningitis</a> using remote sensing technology; to provide the technology needed to bring education to all parts of the country through distant learning, and to aid in conflict resolution and border disputes by mapping out state and International borders.
</p><p>NigeriaSat-2, Nigeria's second satellite, was built as a high-resolution earth satellite by Surrey Space Technology Limited, a United Kingdom-based satellite technology company. It has 2.5-metre resolution panchromatic (very high resolution), 5-metre multispectral (high resolution, NIR red, green and red bands), and 32-metre multispectral (medium resolution, NIR red, green and red bands) antennas, with a ground receiving station in <a href="/wiki/Abuja" title="Abuja">Abuja</a>. The NigeriaSat-2 spacecraft alone was built at a cost of over £35 million. This satellite was launched into <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a> by a military base in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup> On 10 November 2008 (0900 GMT), the satellite was reportedly switched off for analysis and to avoid a possible collision with other satellites. According to Nigerian Communications Satellite Limited, it was put into "emergency mode operation to effect mitigation and repairs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup> The satellite eventually failed after losing power on 11 November 2008. On 24 March 2009, the Nigerian Federal Ministry of Science and Technology, NigComSat Ltd. and CGWIC signed another contract for the in-orbit delivery of the NigComSat-1R satellite. NigComSat-1R was also a DFH-4 satellite, and the replacement for the failed NigComSat-1 was successfully launched into orbit by China in Xichang on 19 December 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup> The satellite was stated to have a positive impact on national development in various sectors such as communications, internet services, health, agriculture, environmental protection and national security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nigeria_EduSat-1" title="Nigeria EduSat-1">NigeriaEduSat-1</a> was a satellite designed, built, and owned by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_University_of_Technology_Akure" title="Federal University of Technology Akure">Federal University of Technology Akure</a> (FUTA), in conjunction with Nigeria's National Space Research and Development Agency and Japan's Kyushu Institute of Technology. It was equipped with 0.3-megapixel and 5-megapixel cameras, and with the rest of the satellite, the fleet took images of Nigeria. The satellite transmitted songs and poems as an outreach project to generate Nigerian interest in science. The signal could be received by <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_operator" title="Amateur radio operator">amateur radio operators</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Satellite_constellation" title="Satellite constellation">satellite constellation</a> also conducted measurements of the atmospheric density 400 kilometres (1.3 million feet) above the Earth. The satellite cost about US$500,000 to manufacture and launch.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_affairs">Social affairs</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Nigeria" title="Health in Nigeria">Health in Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paediatric_ward,_General_hospital,_Ilorin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Paediatric_ward%2C_General_hospital%2C_Ilorin.jpg/220px-Paediatric_ward%2C_General_hospital%2C_Ilorin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Paediatric_ward%2C_General_hospital%2C_Ilorin.jpg/330px-Paediatric_ward%2C_General_hospital%2C_Ilorin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Paediatric_ward%2C_General_hospital%2C_Ilorin.jpg/440px-Paediatric_ward%2C_General_hospital%2C_Ilorin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paediatric_ward,_General_hospital,_Ilorin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Paediatric ward, General hospital, Ilorin.</div></div></div>
<p>Health care delivery in Nigeria is a concurrent responsibility of the three tiers of government in the country, and the private sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup> Nigeria has been reorganising its health system since the <a href="/wiki/Bamako_Initiative" title="Bamako Initiative">Bamako Initiative</a> of 1987, which formally promoted community-based methods of increasing accessibility of drugs and health care services to the population, in part by implementing user fees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup> The new strategy dramatically increased accessibility through community-based health care reform, resulting in more efficient and equitable provision of services. A comprehensive approach strategy was extended to all areas of health care, with subsequent improvement in the health care indicators and improvement in health care efficiency and cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS_in_Nigeria" title="HIV/AIDS in Nigeria">HIV/AIDS rate in Nigeria</a> is much lower compared to the other African nations such as Botswana or South Africa whose prevalence (percentage) rates are in the double digits. As of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nigeria&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> prevalence rate among adults ages 15–49 was 1.5 per cent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-285">[285]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> in Nigeria is 54.7 years on average,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_285-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-285">[285]</a></sup> and 71% and 39% of the population have access to improved water sources and improved <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup> As of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nigeria&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the infant mortality is 74.2 deaths per 1,000 <a href="/wiki/Live_birth_(human)" title="Live birth (human)">live births</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2012, a new bone marrow donor program was launched by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Nigeria" title="University of Nigeria">University of Nigeria</a> to help people with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leukaemia" title="Leukaemia">leukaemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lymphoma" title="Lymphoma">lymphoma</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Sickle_cell_disease" title="Sickle cell disease">sickle cell disease</a> to find a compatible donor for a life-saving <a href="/wiki/Hematopoietic_stem_cell_transplantation" title="Hematopoietic stem cell transplantation">bone marrow transplant</a>, which cures them of their conditions. Nigeria became the second African country to have successfully carried out this surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McNeil_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-McNeil-288">[288]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Western_African_Ebola_virus_epidemic" title="Western African Ebola virus epidemic">2014 Ebola outbreak</a>, Nigeria was the first country to effectively contain and eliminate the Ebola threat that was ravaging three other countries in the West African region; the unique method of <a href="/wiki/Contact_tracing" title="Contact tracing">contact tracing</a> employed by Nigeria became an effective method later used by countries such as the United States when Ebola threats were discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matt_Schiavenza_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matt_Schiavenza-289">[289]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Punch_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Punch-290">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vanguard_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vanguard-291">[291]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Nigerian health care system is continuously faced with a shortage of doctors known as "<a href="/wiki/Human_capital_flight" title="Human capital flight">brain drain</a>", because of emigration by skilled Nigerian doctors to North America and Europe. In 1995, an estimated 21,000 Nigerian doctors were practising in the United States alone, which is about the same as the number of doctors working in the Nigerian public service. Retaining these expensively trained professionals has been identified as one of the goals of the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[292]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Nigeria" title="Education in Nigeria">Education in Nigeria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:University_of_Lagos2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="986" decoding="async" height="92" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/University_of_Lagos2.jpg/220px-University_of_Lagos2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/University_of_Lagos2.jpg/330px-University_of_Lagos2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/University_of_Lagos2.jpg/440px-University_of_Lagos2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:University_of_Lagos2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Lagos" title="University of Lagos">University of Lagos</a></div></div></div>
<p>Education in Nigeria is overseen by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Ministry_of_Education_(Nigeria)" title="Federal Ministry of Education (Nigeria)">Ministry of Education</a>. <a href="/wiki/Local_government_areas_of_Nigeria" title="Local government areas of Nigeria">Local authorities</a> take responsibility for implementing policy for state-controlled public education and state schools at a regional level. The education system is divided into <a href="/wiki/Kindergarten" title="Kindergarten">kindergarten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Primary_education" title="Primary education">primary education</a>, <a href="/wiki/Secondary_education" title="Secondary education">secondary education</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tertiary_education" title="Tertiary education">tertiary education</a>. After the 1970s oil boom, tertiary education was improved so it would reach every subregion of Nigeria. 68% of the Nigerian population is literate, and the rate for men (75.7%) is higher than that for women (60.6%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CP2006_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-CP2006-293">[293]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nigeria provides free, government-supported education, but attendance is not compulsory at any level, and certain groups, such as nomads and the handicapped, are under-served. The education system consists of six years of primary school, three years of junior secondary school, three years of senior secondary school, and four, five or six years of university education leading to a bachelor's degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CP2006_293-1"><a href="#cite_note-CP2006-293">[293]</a></sup> The government has majority control of university education. Tertiary education in Nigeria consists of universities (public and private), polytechnics, monotechnics, and colleges of education. The country has a total of 138 universities, with 40 federally owned, 39 state-owned, and 59 privately owned. Nigeria was ranked 118th in the <a href="/wiki/Global_Innovation_Index" title="Global Innovation Index">Global Innovation Index</a> in 2021, down from 114th in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[294]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crime">Crime</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Crime_in_Nigeria" title="Crime in Nigeria">Crime in Nigeria</a></div>
